The Necessity of Conciseness in Teaching Theology
Sanusi argues for conciseness and defines three rhetorical styles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why conciseness (ikhtisar) is necessary in teaching theology, and the distinctions between prolixity (tatwil), brevity (ijaz), and conciseness.

Core Idea
Conciseness is obligatory because without it, teaching fails both the weak and the strong.

Now the author explains his methodology: he writes concisely. He argues that both excessive verbosity (tatwil) and excessive brevity (ijaz) that obscures meaning are blameworthy. Only a balanced conciseness (ikhtisar) achieves the goal of teaching. The footnote clarifies the rhetorical terms. Let's unpack this argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the argument for conciseness
Read the passage from 'فَصَارَ فِيهِ' to 'فهو واجب'. Notice that the author cites two extremes and then concludes that conciseness is obligatory.
The logic is: verbosity tires weak students, poor brevity fails to teach, so only conciseness works.
2
Learn the three key terms
The footnote defines tatwil, ijaz, and ikhtisar. Identify each definition and distinguish ikhtisar from pure brevity (ijaz).
Tatwil includes both hashw (useless padding) and itnab (beneficial elaboration). Ijaz is less than customary.
3
See the author's balanced approach
The commentator notes that the author intended to cover major topics briefly without excessive branching. Reflect on how this balances the extremes.
Conciseness here means delivery with few words without compromising the intended meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جمع همة ، وهي لغة: القوَّة والعزم. وعرفا: حالةٌ للنَّفس تتبعها قوَّة إرادة ، وغلبة انبعاث، إلى نَيْل مقصودِ مَا ، ثُمَّ إِنْ تعلَّقتْ بمعالي الأمور فهي عَليَّةٌ ، وإلا فدنيَّة. (فَصَارَ فِيهِ) أي: في تعليم أصول الدين بالتأليف (الِاخْتِصَارُ) أي الإيجاز، وهو: تقليل اللفظ ، ضدُّ التَّطويل . (مُلْتَزَمْ) تقريباً على المتعلمين القاصرين ؛ فظهر من كلام المصنف رحمه الله تعالى منطوقا ومفهوما .. أنَّ الإطناب المُمِلَّ مذموم ؛ لأنَّه يمنع الهمم القاصرة من تعاطيه، والإيجاز المُخلَّ بأداء المقصود كذلك ؛ لأنَّه لا يوصِلُ إلى صحة فهمه، فيتعيَّن الاختصارُ ؛ لأنَّ ما لا يتمُّ الواجب إلا به فهو واجب (۱). وَهَذِهِ أُرْجُوزَةٌ لَقَبْتُها جَوْهَرَةَ التَّوْحِيْدِ قَدْ هَذَّبتها (1) ههنا ألفاظ ذكرها المصنّف والشَّارحُ رحمهما الله تعالى يجب معرفة حقائقها، وهذه الألفاظ هي: التطويل، والإيجاز، والاختصار، وأما التطويل فهو: أداء المقصود بلفظ زائد على المتعارف لأوساط الناس الذين ليس لهم فصاحة ولا بلاغة ، وينبغي أن يراد به ههنا ما يَشمل الذي سماه أهل البلاغة حشوا وإطناباً ، فأمَّا الحشو :فهو ما تعيَّنت زيادته على أداء أصل المقصود ولم تكن له فائدةٌ وأمَّا الإطناب فهو : الزِّيادة عن أصل المطلوب لفائدة . وأما الإيجاز فهو: أداء المقصود بلفظ أقلّ مِن المُتعارف ، وقد ذم المصنف التطويل صراحة بأن جعل الهمم تَضْعُفُ عن التحصيل بسببه ، وذم الإيجاز المُخلَّ بالمفهوم ؛ لأنَّ الإيجاز المُخلَّ بالمقصودِ لا تبيين معه ، وقد ذكرَ أنَّ هذا العلم يحتاجُ للتّبيين ، فلم يبق إلا الاختصار ، وهو : ههنا بمعونة المقام - عبارة عن أداء المعنى المقصود بعبارة قليلة من غير إخلال بشيء من المطلوب أداؤُه ، ويشبه أن يكون قد أراد الاقتصار على أمهات المسائل، وترك التفريعات الكثيرة ، وأداء ذلك بعبارة قصيرة سهلة الفهم. محي.
He gathered determination (himma), which in language means strength and resolve. And in conventional terminology: a state of the soul accompanied by strength of will and predominance of impulse towards attaining some objective. Then if it is attached to lofty matters it is high, otherwise it is low. (Thus became in it) i.e. in teaching the principles of religion through authorship (the conciseness) i.e. brevity, which is: reducing the wording, the opposite of prolixity. (Is incumbent) approximately for deficient students. So it appears from the speech of the author, may Allah have mercy on him, explicitly and implicitly, that tiresome verbosity is blameworthy because it prevents deficient determinations from undertaking it, and that brevity which compromises the delivery of the intended meaning is likewise blameworthy because it does not lead to correct understanding. So conciseness is necessary, because that without which an obligatory act is not completed is itself obligatory. And this is a poem (urjuzah) which I have titled Jawharat al-Tawhid, which I have refined. (1) Here are terms mentioned by the author and the commentator, may Allah have mercy on them, whose realities must be known. These terms are: prolixity (tatwil), brevity (ijaz), and conciseness (ikhtisar). As for prolixity, it is: delivering the intended meaning with wording exceeding the customary for average people who have neither eloquence nor rhetoric. It should be understood here as including what the scholars of rhetoric call 'padding' (hashw) and 'elaboration' (itnab). As for 'padding': it is what is determined as extra beyond delivering the original intended meaning and has no benefit. As for 'elaboration': it is the addition beyond the original required meaning for a benefit. As for brevity (ijaz), it is: delivering the intended meaning with wording less than customary. The author explicitly censured prolixity by stating that determinations become weak in acquiring [knowledge] because of it, and he censured brevity that compromises understanding because brevity that compromises the intended meaning has no clarity with it. He mentioned that this science requires clarification, so nothing remains except conciseness (ikhtisar). It is here—with the help of context—an expression for delivering the intended meaning with few words without compromising anything of what is required to be delivered. It resembles that he intended to confine to the main issues and leave aside the many ramifications, and to deliver that with short, easy-to-understand phrasing. Muhi.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage begins by defining the term 'himma' (determination/ambition) both linguistically and terminologically. It then transitions to the commentary on the poem, explaining that the author has adopted a concise style (ikhtisar) in teaching the principles of religion. The passage argues that both excessive verbosity (tatwil) and excessive brevity (ijaz) that compromises meaning are blameworthy. The author then states the verse line of the poem: 'And this is an urjuzah I titled Jawharat al-Tawhid, which I have refined.' A footnote provides detailed definitions of the three key terms: tatwil (prolixity), ijaz (brevity), and ikhtisar (conciseness). The footnote clarifies the subcategories of prolixity (hashw and itnab) and reiterates the necessity of conciseness that does not compromise the intended meaning. The commentator notes that the author likely aimed to cover major topics briefly without extensive branching.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All brevity is good because it saves time.
Correction: Brevity that compromises understanding is blameworthy, as it fails to convey the intended meaning.

Checkpoint
Why does the author consider conciseness (ikhtisar) obligatory?
A) Because verbosity is a sin.
B) Because it is the shortest style.
C) Because without it, the duty of teaching cannot be fulfilled.
D) Because the students are lazy.

Key Takeaway
Conciseness (ikhtisar) is the balanced middle path that makes teaching effective for all learners.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you explained a religious matter at great length, only to see your listener become bored or confused. You now know that prolixity is blameworthy. Will you commit to cutting unnecessary words in your future teaching, even if it feels unnatural?
?
The lesson warns that brevity that compromises meaning is also blameworthy. When you summarize a belief for someone, do you ever sacrifice accuracy for simplicity? How can you ensure your summaries are concise yet faithful?
?
Conciseness (ikhtisar) delivers the intended meaning without compromise. In your own journey of learning 'aqeedah, have you ever accepted a version of a doctrine that was too short to be clear? What responsibility does that place on you to seek deeper, clearer sources?

Description and Naming of the Poem Jawharat al-Tawhid
Sanusi introduces the poem's form and encourages its study.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the physical description of the poem Jawharat al-Tawhid, the reasoning behind its name, and the threefold encouragement to study it.

Core Idea
A concise 144-line arjuzah jewel of Tawhid, designed for easy memorization.

This passage from Sanusi's introduction provides the essential facts about the poem: its meter, size, and title. It also includes a brief theological-linguistic discussion about how book names work, and concludes with three compelling reasons why students should engage with this work. Understanding these details sets the stage for the doctrinal content that follows.

Guided Path
1
Identify the poem's key features
Read the first part of the passage to note the poem's meter (rajaz), size (small), and line count (144).
The term 'arjuzah' itself implies a poem in rajaz meter.
2
Understand the naming
See how the author named it 'Jawharat al-Tawhid' and follow the debate about whether book names are proper nouns for genera or personal proper nouns. Sanusi adopts the latter view.
This debate is a side point; focus on the name.
3
Analyze the three encouragements
The passage lists three reasons to study the poem: it is poetry (easier to memorize than prose), in rajaz meter (simplest meter), and small in size (encourages engagement). List and reflect on each.
These reasons show pedagogical intentionality.
4
Connect to your own learning
Consider how these features affect your own study of the poem. Does the small size make it less daunting? Does the meter help memorization?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على وجه مخصوص (أَرْجُوزَةٌ) أي: منظومة من بحر الرجز(١) ، صغيرة الحجم ، أبياتها أربعة وأربعونَ ومئةَ بيتٍ ؛ ففيه ترغيب في تعاطيها (٢)، وأكده بقوله: (لَقَبْتُها) أي: جعلتُ لها (جوهرة) عِلْم (التوحيد) لقبًا، والجوهرة: مبني أنَّ وحاصله: أنا لا نسلم أنَّ الأرجوزة اسم لما في ذهن المصنف وغيره؛ لأن هذا أسماء الكتب من قبيل أعلام الأجناس ، أو أسماء الأجناس ، ونحن لا نقول به ، بل الأرجوزة اسم لما في ذهن المصنف وحده بناء على أنَّ أسماء الكتب من قبيل الأعلام الشخصية، وهو ما نختاره. (٢) وقد أشار إلى الترغيب فيها من ثلاثة أوجه: أولها أنها نظم، فإنَّ المعلوم أنَّ النَّظم أعذب، وأعلى، وأقرب إلى الحفظ والاستظهار من النثر. والثاني: أنها من بحر الرجز، وبحر الرجز أيسر وأسهل من غيره من البحور. والثالث: أنها صغيرة الحجم فإن كلمة (أرجوزة) تدلّ عرفًا على قلة عدد الأبيات، وذلك أدعى إلى الإقبال عليها والعمل على استيعابها حفظا وفهما. محي.
In a specific manner: (Arjuzah), meaning: a poem in the meter of rajaz, small in size, its lines are one hundred and forty-four; so there is an encouragement in engaging with it. And he emphasized it by saying: (I have entitled it) meaning: I have made for it (Jawharah) of the science of Tawhid as a title. And (the commentator says: ) the summary is: we do not concede that arjuzah is a name for what is in the author's mind and others', because these names of books are of the type of proper nouns for genera or generic nouns, and we do not say that, but rather arjuzah is a name for what is in the author's mind alone, based on the view that names of books are of the type of personal proper nouns, and that is what we choose. (2) And he pointed to encouragement in it from three aspects: first, that it is poetry, for it is known that poetry is sweeter, higher, and closer to memorisation and retention than prose. Second, that it is in the meter of rajaz, and the rajaz meter is easier and simpler than other meters. Third, that it is small in size, for the word 'arjuzah' conventionally indicates a small number of lines, and that is more conducive to turning towards it and working to grasp it memorisation and understanding. Muhyi.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage describes the poem itself. It states that the poem is an 'arjuzah'—a poem in the rajaz meter—small in size, containing 144 lines. The author emphasizes encouragement in studying it. Then the passage explains the title: the author named it 'Jawharat al-Tawhid' (The Jewel of Tawhid). Following that, there is a note (footnote 2) elaborating three reasons for encouragement: first, poetry is more appealing and easier to memorise than prose; second, the rajaz meter is the easiest poetic meter; third, the small size (implied by 'arjuzah') motivates learners to engage with it fully. Note that the intervening text about 'we do not concede...' seems to be a continuation of the previous objection discussion, possibly misplaced, but it is included as per the OCR.

بحر الرجز
جوهرة التوحيد
نظم

حفظ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The small size means the content is simple or easy to understand without effort.
Correction: The text explicitly says the small size is to encourage engagement, not that the content is simple. The doctrines are profound and dense.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Jawharat al-Tawhid is a concise 144-line poem in rajaz meter, named to reflect its jewel-like value, and its design encourages easy memorization.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author designed this poem to be small and in an easy meter to encourage memorization. What small but valuable piece of Islamic knowledge have you neglected because you thought you had no time? How can you apply the principle of 'small size, easy meter' to your own learning habits starting today?
?
Sanusi says poetry is 'sweeter, higher, and closer to memorisation' than prose. Have you ever used poetry or songs to memorize something important? If not, what is one verse or hadith you could set to a simple rhythm this week to ensure you never forget it?
?
The poem is called 'The Jewel of Tawhid'. Think of the most valuable thing you own. Do you treasure Allah's oneness more than that? How does this poem's status as a 'jewel' challenge you to prioritize understanding Tawhid over other pursuits?

The Obligation to Know Allah and Conditions of Taklif
The commentator defines taklif, lists its conditions, and discusses obligation for those unreached by the call.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what taklif (religious responsibility) means, who is obligated to know Allah, and the conditions that must be met for that obligation.

Core Idea
Taklif is the foundation of Islamic responsibility, binding only those with maturity, reason, and the prophetic call.

This passage from the commentary on Jawharat al-Tawhid explains a key verse about the obligation to know Allah. The commentator defines taklif, lists its conditions, and distinguishes between humans and jinn. By understanding this, you will grasp who is required to seek knowledge of Allah and the scope of that duty.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and its explanation
Read the opening verse and the commentator's explanation carefully. The verse states that every mukallaf must know what is necessary, possible, and impossible for Allah and His messengers.
This is the central claim being explained.
2
Define taklif and the mukallaf
Note the two definitions of taklif: (1) imposing something involving hardship and (2) requesting something involving hardship. Also note the definition of mukallaf: an adult, rational person who has received the call.
The second definition covers all five categories of rulings.
3
Examine the conditions of taklif
Identify the three main conditions: puberty, reason, and hearing the call. Consider why a fourth condition (sound senses) is implied. Also note that jinn are obligated from creation without needing puberty.
4
Understand the categories of rulings
Link the two definitions to the five rulings: obligation, prohibition, recommendation, dislike, and permission (implied). See how they arise from decisive/non-decisive requests for action or abandonment.
This shows the practical outcome of taklif.
5
Reflect on who is excluded
Think about those not obligated: children before puberty, the insane, and those who have not received the call. Also, angels are not mukallaf in this sense because their knowledge is innate.
This clarifies the scope of the obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجوب معرفة الله على المكلف، وحكم من لم تبلغه الدعوة]
وجوبُ معرفة الله على المكلف، وحكم من لم تبلغه الدعوة]
فَكُلُّ مَنْ كُلِّفَ شَرْعًا وَجَبَا عَلَيْهِ أَنْ يَعْرِفَ مَا قَدْ وَجَبَا ١٠ للهِ والْجَائِزَ والمُمْتَنعَا وَمِثْل ذَا لِرُسُلِهِ فَاسْتَمِعا
فَكُلُّ مَنْ كُلِّف) مِن الثَّقلين (٢) ، والتَّكليف : إلزام ما فيه كُلفة (٣)
والمُكلَّف: هو البالغ العاقل الذي بلغته (٤) الدعوة ، فمن لم تبلغه
(١) قوله : (لا لرياء ولا غيره) ساقط من المطبوع . (٢) المراد بالثقلين الإنس والجن، دون الملائكة ، أمَّا على مذهب من قال: (إن الملائكة غير المكلفين أصلا فالأمر ظاهر ، وأما على مذهب من قال: «إن الملائكة مكلفون» ؛ فلأن قائل هذا إنما أراد أنهم مكلفون بالنسبة إلى غير معرفة الله ؛ لأن معرفة الله تعالى جبلية فيهم فليس فيهم من يجهل صفاته تعالى كما في الإنس والجن، ألا ترى إلى قوله سبحانه: شَهِدَ اللَّهُ أَنَّهُ لَا إِلَهَ إِلَّا هُوَ وَالْمَلَتَبِكَةُ وَأُولُوا الْعِلْمِ . محي.
(٣) هذا أحد تعريفين للتكليف والثَّاني أنه طلب ما فيه كلفة . فإنْ فَسَّرته بما فسره به الشارح شمل نوعين فقط ، وهما: الوجوب والتحريم ؛ لأنَّ في الواجب إلزام فعل، وفي الحرام إلزام ترك ، وإنْ فَسَّرْتَه بالثَّاني الذي ذكرناه شمل هذين والندب والكراهة، وذلك لأن الطلب إما أن يكون طلب فعل ، وإما أن يكون طلب ترك ، وكلّ واحدٍ من هذين إما أن يكون جازما ، وإما أن يكون غير جازم، فإن كان طلب فعل جازمًا فهو الوجوب ، وإن كان طلب فعل غير جازم فهو الندب ، وإن كان طلب ترك جازماً فهو التَّحريم ، وإن كان طلب ترك غير جازم فهو الكراهة . محي.
(٤) ذكر الشَّارحُ ثلاثة شروط للتكليف، وهي: البلوغ، والعقل، وبلوغ دعوة الرسول، وتَرَكَ رابعا ، وهو سلامة الحواس ؛ مَع أنه ذكر مُحترزَه في كلام الحافظ ابن حجر ، وهذه الشروط معتبرة في تكليف الإنس؛ فأمَّا الجن فإنَّهم مكلفون من أصل الخلقة ؛ فلا يتوقف تكليفهم على البلوغ.
[The obligation to know Allah for the mukallaf, and the ruling on one whom the call has not reached] The obligation to know Allah for the mukallaf, and the ruling on one whom the call has not reached: 'For everyone who is obligated (mukallaf) legally, it becomes obligatory upon him to know what is necessary for Allah, the possible, and the impossible, and likewise for His messengers, so listen.' So 'everyone who is obligated' from the two heavy ones (al-thaqalayn: humans and jinn). And taklif is: imposing something involving hardship. The mukallaf is the adult, rational person whom the call has reached. So one whom the call has not reached... (1) His statement: 'not for ostentation nor otherwise' is omitted from the print. (2) The two heavy ones refer to humans and jinn, not angels. As for the view that angels are not obligated originally, the matter is clear. And as for the view that angels are obligated, the proponent only meant they are obligated with respect to matters other than knowledge of Allah, because knowledge of Allah is innate in them; there is none among them who is ignorant of His attributes as is the case with humans and jinn. Do you not see His statement, may He be glorified: 'Allah bears witness that there is no deity except Him, and the angels, and those of knowledge.' - Muhyi. (3) This is one of two definitions of taklif; the second is that it is a request involving hardship. If you interpret it as the commentator did, it includes only two types: obligation and prohibition, because the obligatory necessitates performance and the forbidden necessitates abandonment. If you interpret it by the second definition we mentioned, it includes these two plus recommendation and dislike. That is because request is either for an action or for an abandonment. Each of these two is either decisive or non-decisive. If it is a decisive request for action, it is obligation (wujub); if non-decisive request for action, it is recommendation (nadb); if decisive request for abandonment, it is prohibition (tahrim); if non-decisive request for abandonment, it is dislike (karaha). - Muhyi. (4) The commentator mentioned three conditions for taklif: puberty, reason, and hearing the call of the messenger. He omitted a fourth, which is soundness of the senses, even though he mentioned its exclusion in the words of Ibn Hajar. These conditions are considered for the taklif of humans. As for jinn, they are obligated from the original creation; their obligation does not depend on puberty.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage defines the obligation to know Allah and explains the concept of taklif (religious responsibility). It begins with a verse from the Jawharat al-Tawhid stating that every legally obligated person must know what is necessary, possible, and impossible for Allah and His messengers. The commentator then defines taklif as 'imposing something involving hardship' and states that the mukallaf is the adult, rational person who has received the prophetic call. In footnotes, he clarifies: (1) The two heavy ones (thaqalayn) are humans and jinn, excluding angels; angels' knowledge is innate. (2) There are two definitions of taklif: the first (imposing something with hardship) includes only obligation and prohibition; the second (request involving hardship) includes also recommendation and dislike. (3) Three conditions for human taklif: puberty, reason, and hearing the call; a fourth (sound senses) is mentioned by Ibn Hajar. Jinn are obligated from creation without waiting for puberty.

Key Terms
التكليف (taklif)
المكلَّف (mukallaf)
الثقلين (al-thaqalayn)
البلوغ (puberty)
العقل (reason)
الدعوة (the call)
الوجوب (wujub: obligation)
التحريم (tahrim: prohibition)
الندب (nadb: recommendation)
الكراهة (karaha: dislike)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think angels are also mukallaf in the same way as humans, obligated to seek knowledge of Allah.
Correction: The passage explains that angels' knowledge of Allah is innate; they are not expected to acquire it. Their obligation (if any) pertains to other matters.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT a condition for taklif for humans?
A) Puberty.
B) Reason.
C) Hearing the call of the messenger.
D) Being free from sin.

Key Takeaway
Taklif imposes the duty to know Allah upon every mature, rational human and jinn who have received the call, defined by specific conditions and categories of rulings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know that you are a mukallaf because you are mature, rational, and have heard the call of Islam. But have you ever really considered that your knowledge of Allah and His messengers is not optional but a binding obligation? What specific steps will you take today to ensure you are fulfilling this duty, not just assuming you know enough?
?
The passage distinguishes between humans and jinn, and excludes angels. Reflect: Your obligation is not innate like the angels; you must actively seek knowledge. What is one area of your faith (e.g., Allah's attributes, prophethood) that you have been neglecting to learn about? What practical action will you take this week to rectify that?
?
Taklif involves hardship. The very word means 'imposing something involving hardship.' When you find it difficult to learn 'aqeedah or to practice Islam fully, do you complain, or do you accept this as part of the obligation? What is one hardship you have been avoiding, and will you commit to facing it tomorrow for the sake of Allah?

Exemptions from Taklif: Child, Insane, and Drunkard
Sanusi defines who is not mukallaf and why they are not punished.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand who is exempt from the obligation to know Allah and the theological basis for their exemption from punishment.

Core Idea
Not every person is accountable; children, the insane, and non-transgressive drunkards are spared due to Allah's mercy.

This passage from Sanusi's Jawharat al-Tawhid clarifies the scope of religious obligation (taklif). It explains that the call of Islam does not impose liability on certain individuals, and that Allah's mercy ensures they are not punished. We will explore the categories of exemption and the theological rationale behind them.

Guided Path
1
Read and identify the three categories
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Identify the three types of people who are not mukallaf: the child, the insane, and the non-transgressive drunkard.
Notice that the passage also mentions a Hanafi opinion for contrast.
2
Understand the conditions for exemption
Focus on the details: a child dies before puberty is saved even if from disbelievers; the insane is not mukallaf; the drunkard who does not transgress is like the insane, but one who intentionally drinks is accountable.
The passage specifies that if one was rational before insanity or drunkenness, their prior state applies.
3
Reflect on why Allah does not punish them
Read the footnote explaining that Allah, though not obliged, does not punish such people because of His mercy. He does not test us with what confounds reason.
The verse from al-Busiri illustrates this mercy.
4
Understand the logical connection
Note that the conjunction 'He does not punish' is a necessary consequence of the lack of obligation: if there is no obligation, there is no punishment.
This is highlighted in al-Shanwani's commentary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجوب معرفة الله على المكلف الدعوة (۱) لا يجب عليه ما ذُكر على الأصح ، ولا يُعذِّبُ (۲) ، : وخرج بالبالغ: الصبي فليس الصبي مكلفا ؛ فمن مات قبل البلوغ فهو ناج، ولو كان من أولاد الكفار ، ولا يعاقب على كفر ولا غيره، ونُقل عن الحنفيَّة أنهم قالوا: الصبي الذي يعقل مكلف بالإيمان ، فإن اعتقد الإيمان أو الكفر فأمره ظاهر ، وإن لم يعتقد واحدا منهما كان من أهل النار ، لوجوب الإيمان عليه بمجرد العقل. وخرج بالعاقل المجنون فليس بمكلف، ومثله السكران غير المتعدي بسكره، فإن تعدى بسكره كأن تعمد شرب المسكر ، وهو عالم بحاله فهو كالعاقل ، ومحل هذا إذا بلغ مجنونا أو سكرانَ واستمرَّ على ذلك حتّى مات، فإن بلغ عاقلاً ثم جُنَّ أو سكر متعديا ثم مات فهو على ما كان عليه قبل الجنون والسكر. (۲) قوله: (ولا يعذب... الخ) أي: لأن الله تعالى وإن كان لا يسأل عما يفعل .. يفعل في ملكه ما يشاء، لكن بمقتضى سبق رحمته لا يقع منه ما تحتار فيه العقول كل الحيرة، فضلا منه تعالى ، ويرحم الله البوصيري حيث يقول: • محي لَمْ يَمْتَحِنَّا بِمَا تَعْيَا العُقول به حِرْصًا علينا فَلَمْ نَرْتَبْ وَلَمْ نَهِم. الأمير وعند الشنواني: قوله: (لا يعذب ويدخل الجنة لأنّ الله تعالى إن كان لا يسأل عما يفعل= في ملكه ما يشاء، لكن بمقتضى سبق رحمته لا يقع منه ما تحتار فيه العقول كل الحيرة فضلا منه تعالى، وعطفه على ما قبله من عطف اللازم على الملزوم ؛ لأنه يلزم من عدم الوجوب عدم التعذيب.
The obligation of knowing Allah upon the responsible person (mukallaf). The call (da'wa) (1): it is not obligatory upon him what was mentioned according to the most correct (opinion), and He does not punish (2). And by 'adult' is excluded: the child, so the child is not a mukallaf; whoever dies before puberty is saved, even if he was from the children of disbelievers, and he is not punished for disbelief or anything else. And it is narrated from the Hanafis that they said: the child who possesses reason is obligated with faith; if he believes in faith or disbelief, his matter is apparent, and if he does not believe either of them, he is among the people of the Fire, due to the obligation of faith upon him by virtue of reason alone. And by 'rational' is excluded: the insane, so he is not a mukallaf, and similarly the drunkard who does not transgress by his drunkenness. If he transgresses by his drunkenness, such as intentionally drinking intoxicants while knowing his state, he is like the rational. The application of this is if he reaches maturity insane or drunk and continues that way until death. If he reaches maturity rational then becomes insane or drinks intoxicants transgressively and then dies, he is upon what he was before insanity and drunkenness. (2) His statement: 'He does not punish... etc.', meaning: because Allah the Exalted, although He is not asked about what He does... He does in His kingdom what He wills, but by virtue of the precedence of His mercy, nothing occurs from Him about which minds are utterly perplexed, as a favor from Him the Exalted. And may Allah have mercy on al-Busiri where he says: 'He did not test us with what minds are incapable of, out of care for us, so we did not doubt and did not become confused.' Al-Amir. And according to al-Shanwani: His statement: 'He does not punish and enters Paradise because Allah the Exalted, although He is not asked about what He does... in His kingdom what He wills, but by virtue of the precedence of His mercy, nothing occurs from Him about which minds are utterly perplexed, as a favor from Him the Exalted, and his conjunction of it to what precedes it is a conjunction of the necessary to the necessitated; because lack of obligation entails lack of punishment.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage defines who is exempt from the obligation of knowing Allah (mukallaf). It begins with the general rule: the call of Islam is not obligatory upon the person in the sense that he is not punished for not knowing, according to the correct opinion. Then it details three categories: (1) the child—the child is not a mukallaf and is saved even if of disbelieving parents. The Hanafi view that a rational child is obliged is mentioned but not adopted. (2) the insane—the insane is not a mukallaf. (3) the drunkard—a drunkard who does not transgress is not a mukallaf, but one who knowingly drinks is like a rational person. Transgression refers to intentional drinking. The rule applies if the condition persists from maturity to death. If one was rational before becoming insane or drunk, he remains accountable for his prior state. The passage then includes a long footnote explaining why Allah does not punish such persons: it is due to His mercy, not obligation. It cites al-Busiri's verse that Allah does not test us with what confounds minds, and al-Shanwani's comment that the conjunction of 'He does not punish' with the previous statement is a conjunction of necessary consequence—no obligation implies no punishment.

صبي
مجنون
سكران
لا يعذب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Allah punishes people even without a valid call or excuse, but the passage clarifies that Allah does not punish children, the insane, or non-transgressive drunkards due to His mercy.
Correction: Allah's mercy dictates that He does not hold accountable those who are incapable or excused by lack of maturity, sanity, or intentional transgression.

Checkpoint

A) He is punished for disbelief if his parents are disbelievers.
B) He is saved, even if from disbelieving parents.

D) He must believe by reason alone.

Key Takeaway
Allah's mercy exempts children, the insane, and non-transgressive drunkards from punishment because obligation is lifted from them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Consider the drunkard who intentionally drinks: he becomes accountable. What does this teach you about personal responsibility? Is there an area in your own life where you have been acting without accountability, assuming mercy without effort? Commit to one change this week that aligns with the seriousness of taklif.

Al-Shanwani's Clarifications on the Six Categories
Al-Shanwani explains the six categories of people exempt from the prophetic call.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Shanwani's precise definitions of six categories of people who are excused from religious accountability due to lack of understanding or absence of prophetic message.

Core Idea
Al-Shanwani refines the hadith's categories to clarify who is truly excused.

Al-Shanwani unpacks each phrase from the hadith, correcting common misunderstandings and providing exact legal definitions. His commentary shows the meticulous nature of Islamic theological-legal reasoning, ensuring that the categories are not misinterpreted as merely physical conditions.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Notice the structure where al-Shanwani addresses each item from the hadith in order.
The passage has three main sections: elderly senile, fatrah, and the correction on akmah.
2
Identify the six categories
The passage ends by listing six distinct categories. Find them: elderly senile, those in the fatrah, born akmah, blind and deaf, born insane, and one who becomes insane before puberty.
Al-Shanwani reclassifies the hadith's items into these six.
3
Understand the definition of akmah
Al-Shanwani explains that 'akmah' here does not mean physically blind but an imbecile without discernment. This is a key correction.
The common misunderstanding is that akmah means blind, but the commentator restricts it to intellectual deficiency.
4
Grasp the significance of fatrah
The term 'fatrah' refers to intervals between prophets. People in these periods are not accountable because no message reached them. This includes the period between Jesus and Muhammad.
Understanding fatrah is crucial for the question of accountability of pre-Islamic peoples.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۳) قوله: (الشيخ الهرم أي : الذي بلغ به الهرم إلى اختلال عقله، ولم تبلغه الدعوة قبل ذلك ، بدليل قوله بَعْدُ : (لو عقلت) ، وإلا فهو إذا كان شيخا هرما عاقلا بلغته الدعوة فهو كغيره من المكلفين . الشنواني . (٤) قوله: (ومن مات في الفترة هم الأمم الكائنون بين أزمنة الرسل، ولم يرسل إليهم الأول ولا أدركم الثاني، فيشمل ما بين محمد وعيسى ، ويشمل العرب الذين بين إسماعيل ونبينا ، والفترة من الفتور ، وهو الغفلة والترك ؛ لأنهم تركوا بلا رسول . (٥) قوله: (أعمى) كان الأولى أن يقول : ومن ولد أكمه أو أعمى أو أصم، فإن الأكمه وحده كان بالمعنى الآتي له ، فالمراد به الأبله الذي لا تمييز عنده، وليس المراد به من طمست عيناه ، فالشيخ الهرم قسم أول ، ومن مات في الفترة قسم ثاني ، ومن ولد أكمه قسم ثالث ، والأعمى والأصم قسم رابع ، ومن ولد مجنونا قسم خامس ، ومن طرأ عليه الجنون قبل أن يبلغ قسم سادس.
(3) His statement: 'the elderly senile' means: one whose senility has reached the point of mental disorder, and the call (da'wah) did not reach him before that, as evidenced by his later statement: 'if only I had understood.' Otherwise, if he is an elderly senile but rational and the call reached him, he is like other morally responsible persons. al-Shanwani. (4) His statement: 'and one who died in the period (fatrah)' are the nations existing between the times of the messengers, to whom neither the first nor the second messenger was sent. It includes what is between Muhammad and Jesus, and includes the Arabs between Ishmael and our Prophet. Al-Fatrah is from fator, which is heedlessness and abandonment, because they were left without a messenger. (5) His statement: 'blind' – it would have been better for him to say: 'and one who was born akmah, or blind, or deaf.' For akmah alone has the meaning to be given later, namely the imbecile who has no discernment; it does not mean one whose eyes are closed. So the elderly senile is the first division, one who died in the fatrah is the second division, one born akmah is the third division, the blind and the deaf are the fourth division, one born insane is the fifth division, and one who becomes insane before puberty is the sixth division.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage is a commentary by al-Shanwani explaining the definitions of the categories mentioned in the hadith. First, he defines the 'elderly senile' as one whose senility has caused mental impairment, and who never received the prophetic call before that point; if the call reached him while rational, he is accountable like other mukallafun. Second, he explains 'those who died in the fatrah' as the people living in intervals between prophets, such as between Jesus and Muhammad, and between Ishmael and Muhammad; the term 'fatrah' implies heedlessness and abandonment because they were left without a messenger. Third, he critiques the wording of the hadith regarding 'akmah (blind)'. He argues that 'akmah' actually refers to an imbecile lacking discernment, not someone physically blind. He then reclassifies the categories into six: (1) elderly senile, (2) fatrah, (3) born akmah (imbecile), (4) blind and deaf, (5) born insane, (6) insane before puberty. This clarification shows the commentator's careful attention to precise theological and legal definitions.

الفترة
الأكمه
الأعمى
الأصم
المجنون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith's term 'akmah' simply means a blind person.
Correction: Al-Shanwani explains that 'akmah' here means an imbecile lacking discernment, not one who is physically blind. The correct categories include separate items for physical blindness and deafness.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Al-Shanwani's commentary clarifies that the hadith's categories are not all about physical disabilities but also intellectual and historical circumstances, ensuring precise theological-legal definitions.

?

The Meaning of al-Akmah in the Hadith
Ibn al-Mubarak defines al-Akmah as one without discernment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that 'al-Akmah' in the hadith refers to intellectual incapacity, not physical blindness.

Core Idea
Al-Akmah is about mental blindness, not sight.

In this brief lesson, we clarify the meaning of 'al-Akmah' as used in a prophetic hadith. Some may mistakenly think it refers to physical blindness, but the commentator Ibn al-Mubarak explains it as a lack of discernment. This distinction is crucial for understanding the hadith's spiritual lesson.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic text and its literal English translation. Notice how the commentator defines 'al-Akmah' and the terms he uses to explain it.
2
Identify the key terms
Look at the three key terms: الأكمه, الأحمق, المعتوه. Notice that the commentator equates al-Akmah with the foolish and imbecile, emphasizing mental incapacity.
3
Reflect on the common misunderstanding
Consider the misconception that al-Akmah means physically blind. The passage corrects this by clarifying it is about not knowing where to turn, i.e., lacking discernment.
This helps in proper understanding of the hadith.

This passage defines the term 'al-Akmah' as used in a hadith. This person is identified with the 'foolish' (al-ahmaq) and the 'imbecile' (al-ma'tuh) who are explicitly mentioned in the hadith. The definition emphasizes the cognitive rather than sensory impairment.

The Definition of Ma'rifa (Knowledge)
Definition of ma'rifa with grammatical and semantic analysis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of ma'rifa as decisive correspondence with reality based on a proof, and its relation to 'ilm.

Core Idea
Ma'rifa is not mere belief; it is certain, proven knowledge.

The commentator begins by defining the central term 'ma'rifa' (knowledge) used in the poem. This definition sets the standard for what counts as valid theological knowledge. Understanding this definition is crucial before proceeding to the arguments that follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the precise wording and try to identify the definition.
Focus on the phrase 'الجزم المطابق للواقع عن دليل'.
2
Understand the grammatical context
The commentator first clarifies the grammatical role of 'ma'rifa' in the poem. See how it is derived and functions as a subject.
The phrase 'في تأويل مصدر فاعل' shows it is treated as a verbal noun.
3
Memorize the definition
Commit the definition to memory: 'decisive correspondence with reality based on a proof'. Note each component: الجزم (certainty), المطابق للواقع (correspondence with reality), عن دليل (based on a proof).
4
Distinguish from mere belief
Recognize that this definition excludes opinions, guesses, or traditions without proof. Only certain knowledge with evidence qualifies as ma'rifa.
This addresses a common misunderstanding.
5
Learn the synonymy with 'ilm
The text notes that the majority equate ma'rifa with 'ilm in meaning, though usage may differ. Understand that both terms refer to certain knowledge.
This clarifies terminology in later texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (معرفة) أشار بذلك إلى أن أن والفعل في تأويل مصدر فاعل (وجبا عليه). والمعرفة: هي الجزم المطابق للواقع عن دليل. والجمهور أن العلم والمعرفة بمعنى واحد وإن اختلفا في الاستعمال.
His statement: (knowledge) indicated thereby that 'that' and the verb are in the interpretation of a verbal noun as subject of 'became obligatory upon him'. And knowledge is: decisive correspondence with reality from a proof. And the majority [hold] that 'ilm and ma'rifa have one meaning, even if they differ in usage.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage is a definition of the term 'معرفة' (ma'rifa). It first explains the grammatical function: the word 'معرفة' is the source (masdar) of the verb 'يعرف' and stands as the subject of 'وجبا عليه'. Then it gives the semantic definition: ma'rifa is 'decisive correspondence with reality based on a proof'—meaning it is certain knowledge that matches reality and is supported by evidence. Finally, it notes that the majority of scholars consider 'ilm and ma'rifa to be synonymous in meaning, although they may differ in usage (e.g., ma'rifa often implies experiential knowledge). The point is to clarify that the term in the poem is not merely 'belief' but certain knowledge.

Key Terms
المعرفة
العلم
الجزم
المطابق للواقع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think ma'rifa is mere belief or opinion without proof.
Correction: Ma'rifa requires decisive certainty (الجزم), correspondence with reality, and a proof (دليل). It is not mere belief.

Checkpoint

A) Any belief without proof
B) Decisive correspondence with reality from a proof
C) Intuition without evidence
D) Tradition-based knowledge

Key Takeaway
Ma'rifa is defined as certain knowledge that corresponds to reality and is based on proof, and it is synonymous with 'ilm.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

Definition of the Muqallid and His Faith
Shanwani defines the muqallid and states that his faith is not free from doubt.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Shanwani defines the muqallid and why his faith is considered problematic because it lacks proof and is accompanied by doubt.

Core Idea
The muqallid's faith lacks proof and is tainted by doubt.

Before we can assess the validity of a person's faith, we must understand what it means to be a muqallid—one who accepts beliefs without independent proof. Al-Shanwani gives a clear definition and then draws a crucial conclusion: such faith is not free from hesitation. This passage establishes the initial ruling that will be refined later.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Notice how al-Shanwani defines the muqallid and then describes his faith.
Pay attention to the terms: تقليد, مقلد, إيمان, تردد.
2
Identify the definition of the muqallid
Find the phrase where al-Shanwani explains what it means to follow another. Notice that he mentions taking statements without proof and without reflection on creation.
The muqallid simply accepts from someone else without evidence.
3
Identify the ruling on his faith
The passage states that the muqallid's faith is not free from hesitation. Understand why: it lacks proof, so doubt accompanies it.
This contradicts iman if iman is defined as knowledge or inner speech that is certain.
4
Consider the nuance at the end
The passage ends by noting that the absolute or detailed ruling depends on whether the muqallid is qualified for independent reflection. This hints at further discussion.
Not all muqallids are treated the same; their situation matters.

ولو إجمالية و(قَلَّدَ) غيره : أي أخذ بقوله (في) أحكام (التَّوْحِيدِ) يعني عِلْمٍ العقائدِ الإسلاميَّةِ مِن غير حُجَّةٍ (۱) ، ولا تفكر في خَلْق السَّماوات والأرض (إِيمَانُهُ ) أي : جَزْمُه بما أخذَه من أحكام التَّوحيد من غيره بلا دليل عليه (لَمْ يَخْلُ) أي: لا يَسْلَمُ مِنْ تَرْدِيدِ) أي: تردُّدٍ وتحير، بل هو مصحوب به، وذلكَ يُنافي الإيمان بناءً على أنَّه : نفسُ المعرفة (٢) ، أو حديث النَّفس التَّابعُ يفصل كما يأتي ، ولا يأتي الإطلاق والتفصيل إلا إذا كان الموضوع: المقلد من حيث هو متأهلا أو لا . الشنواني.
And [the verse] 'or general' and 'he followed' another: meaning he took his statement in the rulings of tawhid, i.e., the science of Islamic creeds, without proof, and without reflection on the creation of the heavens and the earth. 'His faith' means: his conviction of what he took from the rulings of tawhid from another without evidence for it, 'is not free from hesitation' meaning: uncertainty and confusion; rather it is accompanied by it. And that contradicts iman based on the fact that it is [defined as] knowledge itself, or the inner speech that follows it as will come, and the absolute and detailed [ruling] only comes when the subject is the muqallid in terms of whether he is qualified or not. Al-Shanwani.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage is a definition and initial ruling. It explains the meaning of the verse about the muqallid. First, it defines a muqallid as one who takes the rulings of tawhid from someone else without proof or reflection. Then it defines 'his faith' as his conviction without evidence. It states that such faith is not free from hesitation and confusion; rather it is accompanied by doubt. This contradicts the essence of iman if iman is defined as knowledge or as the inner speech that follows it. The passage ends by noting that an absolute or detailed ruling depends on whether the muqallid is qualified for independent reflection or not.

Correction: The passage shows that the muqallid's faith is not free from doubt because it lacks proof, contradicting the essence of iman if iman is defined as knowledge or certain inner speech.

What Is Kalam?

The First Obligatory Duty: Ma'rifat Allah
Defines ma'rifat Allah as the first wajib and clarifies it excludes essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the first obligation upon every accountable person is ma'rifat Allah, which specifically means knowledge of His attributes and the rulings of divinity, not of His essence.

Core Idea
Knowledge of Allah is the first duty, but it is knowledge of His attributes, not His essence.

The poem declares that the very first thing Allah has made obligatory upon every accountable person is ma'rifat Allah. But what does 'knowledge of Allah' mean? The commentator explains that it does not mean trying to comprehend the divine essence — that is impossible. Rather, it means knowing His attributes and the laws of divinity. This lesson unpacks that crucial distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the poetic line and the commentator's explanation.
Focus on the definition of the first obligatory duty.
2
Identify the key distinction
The passage clearly distinguishes between knowledge of Allah's attributes (ma'rifat al-sifat) and knowledge of His essence (dhat). Understand why the latter is excluded.
This distinction is central to Islamic theology.
3
Reflect on the hadith evidence
The commentator cites two hadiths that warn against reflecting on the Creator's essence. Consider how these support the passage's main point.
The hadiths reinforce the idea that Allah's essence is unknowable.

تعيين أول واجب على المكلف وبيان الخلاف فيه
. واجْزِمْ بَأَنَّ أَوَّلًا مِمَّا يَجِبْ
وفيهِ خُلْفٌ مَعْرِفَةٌ وفِيْهِ خُلْفٌ مُنتصب
(وَاجْزم) (۳) اعتقادَكَ أَيُّها المكلَّف بأَنَّ أَوَّلًا مِمَّا يَجِبْ * مَعْرِفَة)
(۱) قوله: (على الوجه السابق) أي في قوله على وجه يقع معه في نفسه أنه عالم بما جزم به فهو وإن كان جازما لكنه لو رجع مقلده يرجع ، ولا يدخل هذا تحت الشاك والظان ؛ لأنهما لا يكفيان ،قطعا ، فمحط النفي قوله: (على الوجه السابق فلا ينافي أن الموضوع أصل الجزم، لكنه يرجع برجوع مقلده، وهذا محمل ما ورد في فتنة القبر: «لا أدري، سمعتُ الناس يقولون شيئا فقلته». الشنواني والأمير (۲) قوله: (في صحة إسلامه) مراده الإسلام المنجي من العذاب الأليم. وقوله: (تترتب عليه أحكامه أي أحكام الآخرة، وإلا فهو يعتبر مسلما في أحكام الدنيا ما لم يظهر منه ما ينافي الإسلام.
(۳) اجزم أي: اعتقد اعتقاداً جازما لا يعتريه تردُّد ولا يطرأ عليه شك، والمخاطب بذلك=
١٦٢
معرفة الله
كلَّ مكلف، من ذكر أو أنثى ، حر أو عبد، إنسي أو جني. واعلم أنَّ سابق الكلام من قوله: فكل من كلّف شرعا وجبا.. ـ الخ» أفاد أنَّ تعالى واجبة على كل مكلف على ما علمتَ ، وقوله هنا واجزم بأنَّ أَوَّلا ... الخ) أفادَ أَنَّ معرفة الله تعالى أول الواجبات على المكلَّف ، وقوله : (بأنَّ أَوَّلا» متعلق بـ«اجزم»، و(من) في قوله: «مما يجب تبعيضية، وقوله: «معرفة» خبر ( أنَّ) ، والتنوين في «معرفة للتعظيم، أو هو عوض عن المضاف إليه ، والأصل معرفة الله» ، ويراد معرفة صفاته سبحانه ، وأحكام الألوهية لا معرفة ذاته وكنه حقيقته ؛ فقد قام الدليل على أنَّه لا يعرف ذاته إلا هو. وفي الحديث: «تفكروا في الخلق ، ولا تفكروا في الخالق ؛ فإنه لا تحيط به الفكرة» ، وفي حديث آخَرَ: «إن الله احتجب عن البصائر كما احتجب عن الأبصار». وبالجملة لا يعرف الله إلَّا الله ؛ فترك الإدراك إدرااً ، والبحث عن ذات الله إشراةٌ ، والمعنى: اعتقد ـ أيها المكلف اعتقاداً جازما لا تردُّد معه ، ولا شكّ أنَّ أوَّل شيءٍ من الواجبات عليك هو معرفة ما الله تعالى.
يجب
Determination of the first obligatory duty upon the accountable person and clarification of the disagreement concerning it. And be firmly certain that the first of what is obligatory is knowledge, and concerning it there is disagreement, and concerning it there is conflict. (And be firmly certain) (3) your conviction, O accountable person, that the first of what is obligatory is knowledge. (1) His saying: (in the aforementioned manner) means: in his saying in a manner in which he finds in himself that he is knowledgeable of what he is convinced of; so even if he is convinced, if his copyist would recant, he recants. And this does not fall under the doubter and the conjecturer, because they are insufficient, absolutely. So the locus of the negation is his saying: (in the aforementioned manner) so it does not contradict that the subject is the basis of conviction, but he recants with the recantation of his copyist. And this is the interpretation of what came in the trial of the grave: 'I do not know; I heard people saying something, so I said it.' (Al-Shanwani and Al-Amir). (2) His saying: (in the validity of his Islam) means the Islam that saves from painful punishment. And his saying: (the application of its rulings upon him) means the rulings of the afterlife; otherwise he is considered a Muslim in the rulings of this world as long as nothing appears from him that contradicts Islam. (3) 'Be firmly certain' means: believe with a firm conviction that is not affected by hesitation nor overtaken by doubt, and the one addressed by that is... 162. Knowledge of Allah. Every accountable person, male or female, free or slave, human or jinn. And know that the preceding speech from his saying: 'For everyone who is obligated by the Shari'a is obliged...' etc. indicated that it (knowledge) is obligatory upon every accountable person as you have learned. And his saying here: 'And be firmly certain that the first...' etc. indicated that knowledge of Allah the Most High is the first of the obligations upon the accountable person. And his saying: (that the first) is connected to 'be firmly certain', and 'min' in his saying 'of what is obligatory' is partitive, and his saying 'knowledge' is the predicate of 'that'. The tanwin on 'knowledge' is for magnification, or it is a substitute for the genitive construct, and the original is 'knowledge of Allah'. And what is intended is knowledge of His attributes, the Most High, and the rulings of divinity, not knowledge of His essence and the reality of His nature; for evidence has established that no one knows His essence except Him. And in the hadith: 'Reflect on the creation, and do not reflect on the Creator, for thought cannot encompass Him.' And in another hadith: 'Indeed Allah has veiled Himself from inner insights as He has veiled Himself from eyesights.' In summary, no one knows Allah except Allah; so refraining from perception is perception itself, and searching into the essence of Allah is polytheism. And the meaning: believe — O accountable person — with a firm conviction, with no hesitation, and no doubt, that the first thing of the obligations upon you is knowledge of what is obligatory concerning Allah the Most High.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation. It begins by quoting a line of the poem: 'And be firmly certain that the first of what is obligatory is knowledge, and concerning it there is disagreement and conflict.' The commentator then provides a detailed explanation. First, he clarifies what it means for a muqallid to have conviction: even if he is initially convinced, if he would recant when his copyist recants, his conviction is not firm enough. He cites the hadith of the trial of the grave as an example. Then he explains that the phrase 'on the aforementioned manner' negates only that specific type of weak conviction, not the principle of conviction itself. He also distinguishes between worldly rulings, where mere verbal affirmation suffices, and afterlife rulings, where true conviction is needed. The commentator then defines 'jazzm' as a firm belief free from hesitation and doubt. The main teaching: the first obligatory duty on every mukallaf (human and jinn) is ma'rifat Allah (knowledge of Allah). This knowledge, however, is of Allah's attributes and the rulings of divinity (ahkam al-uluhiyya), not of His essence (dhat) or the reality of His nature. Proof is given from hadith: 'Reflect on creation, not on the Creator, for thought cannot encompass Him' and 'Allah has veiled Himself from inner insights as He has veiled Himself from eyesights.' The conclusion is that no one knows Allah except Allah; attempting to penetrate His essence is shirk. So the first wajib is to know what is obligatory regarding Allah, i.e., His attributes and what must be believed.

Key Terms

واجب
مكلف
ذات
صفات

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that ma'rifat Allah includes understanding His essence or nature.
Correction: The passage clearly states that ma'rifa is limited to His attributes and the rulings of divinity; His essence is unknowable.

Checkpoint
What does the passage identify as the first obligatory duty on the accountable person?

Key Takeaway
The first obligation upon every accountable person is to know Allah through His attributes and divine laws, not to seek knowledge of His essence, which is beyond human reach.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The lesson says only Allah knows His essence, and any attempt to penetrate it leads to shirk. Is there a part of you that feels frustrated or limited by this boundary? What does that frustration reveal about your trust in Allah's wisdom in veiling Himself from you?

Definition of the First Obligation: Knowledge of God
The author defines knowledge of God as the first obligation and clarifies its meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the first obligation upon every responsible person is knowledge of God, and that this means knowing His attributes and rulings of divinity, not comprehending His essence.

Core Idea
True knowledge of God means knowing His attributes, not grasping His essence.

This lesson establishes the fundamental obligation in Islamic theology: knowledge of God. The author clarifies what this knowledge entails and what it does not, drawing on revelation and reason to set proper boundaries for theological inquiry.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the author's precise language in defining the first obligation.
2
Understand the scope of 'knowledge of God'
The author distinguishes between knowing God's attributes and knowing His essence. Identify the key terms that make this distinction.
The nunation in 'knowledge' indicates magnification.
3
Examine the proof and hadith
The passage cites two hadiths that support the impossibility of knowing God's essence. Reflect on why this is important for avoiding theological error.
The author calls investigation of God's essence an act of associationism (ishrak).
4
Apply the lesson
Consider how this understanding shapes the way a Muslim approaches knowledge of God: focusing on attributes and worship, not speculation about essence.
This guards against both anthropomorphism and extreme agnosticism.

تعيين أول واجب على المكلف وبيان الخلاف فيه
معرفة الله
كلَّ مكلف، من ذكر أو أنثى، حر أو عبد، إنسي أو جني. واعلم أنَّ سابق الكلام من قوله: فكل من كلّف شرعا وجبا.. ـ الخ» أفاد أنَّ تعالى واجبة على كل مكلف على ما علمتَ، وقوله هنا واجزم بأنَّ أَوَّلا ... الخ) أفادَ أَنَّ معرفة الله تعالى أول الواجبات على المكلَّف، وقوله : (بأنَّ أوَّلا» متعلق بـ«اجزم»، و(من) في قوله: «مما يجب تبعيضية، وقوله: «معرفة» خبر ( أنَّ) ، والتنوين في «معرفة للتعظيم، أو هو عوض عن المضاف إليه، والأصل معرفة الله» ، ويراد معرفة صفاته سبحانه ، وأحكام الألوهية لا معرفة ذاته وكنه حقيقته ؛ فقد قام الدليل على أنَّه لا يعرف ذاته إلا هو. وفي الحديث: «تفكروا في الخلق ، ولا تفكروا في الخالق ؛ فإنه لا تحيط به الفكرة» ، وفي حديث آخَرَ: «إن الله احتجب عن البصائر كما احتجب عن الأبصار». وبالجملة لا يعرف الله إلَّا الله ; فترك الإدراك إدرااً ، والبحث عن ذات الله إشراةٌ ، والمعنى: اعتقد ـ أيها المكلف اعتقاداً جازما لا تردُّد معه ، ولا شكّ أنَّ أوَّل شيءٍ من الواجبات عليك هو معرفة ما الله تعالى.
Determining the first obligation upon the responsible person and clarifying the disagreement concerning it. Knowledge of God. Every responsible person, male or female, free or slave, human or jinn. And know that the preceding speech from his saying: 'For everyone who is obligated legally...' indicated that [knowledge of] the Exalted is obligatory upon every responsible person as you have known, and his saying here: 'And be certain that the first...' indicated that knowledge of God, the Exalted, is the first of obligations upon the responsible person. And his saying: 'that the first' is attached to 'be certain', and 'of' in his saying: 'of what is obligatory' is partitive, and his saying: 'knowledge' is the predicate of 'that', and the nunation in 'knowledge' is for magnification, or it is a substitute for the possessed, and the original is 'knowledge of God'. And what is intended is knowledge of His attributes, glory be to Him, and the rulings of divinity, not knowledge of His essence and the reality of His true nature; for proof has established that none knows His essence except Him. And in the hadith: 'Reflect on creation, and do not reflect on the Creator; for thought cannot encompass Him.' And in another hadith: 'Indeed, God has veiled Himself from inner sight just as He has veiled Himself from outer sight.' In summary, none knows God except God; so abandoning perception is perception itself, and investigating the essence of God is an act of associationism. And the meaning: Believe, O responsible person, a firm belief without hesitation, and without doubt, that the first thing from the obligations upon you is knowledge of what is obligatory of God, the Exalted.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage defines the first obligation upon every responsible person according to the author. It begins by stating that the first obligation is knowledge of God (ma'rifat Allah). The author explains that 'knowledge' here is qualified by a nunation that indicates glorification, or it substitutes for the possessed phrase (i.e., 'knowledge of God'). He clarifies that what is meant is knowledge of God's attributes and the rulings of divinity (ahkam al-uluhiyya), not knowledge of His essence (dhat) or the true reality of His nature (kunn haqiqatihi), because proof has shown that none knows His essence but He Himself. Two prophetic hadiths are cited to support this: one commands reflection on creation but forbids reflection on the Creator, and the other states that God is veiled from inner sight just as He is veiled from outer sight. The author concludes that abandoning the attempt to perceive God's essence is itself perception, and seeking to investigate His essence is a form of associationism (ishrak). The passage ends by exhorting the responsible person to have firm belief that the first obligation is knowledge of what is obligatory regarding God.

Definition of Nazar and Its Obligatory Status
Definitions of nazar linguistically and technically, with its ruling and method.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic and technical definition of speculative reasoning (nazar) and why it is obligatory by revelation.

Core Idea
Speculative reasoning begins with self-contemplation and culminates in knowledge of Allah.

This lesson introduces the foundational concept of nazar, the intellectual tool through which we arrive at knowledge of Allah. Sanusi defines it both linguistically and technically, and establishes its obligation by revelation. The passage also hints at the method: starting from oneself, then the cosmos.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two definitions
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the author gives a linguistic definition (sight/thought) and a technical definition (arrangement of matters).
The technical definition is cut off, but the key idea is clear.
2
Contrast the linguistic and technical meanings
The linguistic meaning is everyday sight or thought; the technical meaning is a specific intellectual process of ordering premises to reach a conclusion.
3
Understand the ruling and the method
The passage states that nazar is obligatory by revelation (shar‘), not by reason. This is a distinct Ash‘ari position. The method is: look at yourself, then the upper world, then the lower world.
This method is derived from the Qur’anic command 'fa-nẓur'.
4
Reflect on the pedagogical importance
Why do we need a technical definition? To avoid confusing nazar with mere daydreaming. The arrangement of thoughts must be purposeful and lead to knowledge.
This connects to the broader goal of kalam: systematic reasoning about Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان حقيقة النظر الموصل إلى المعرفة وأنه واجب بالشرع خلافا للمعتزلة. فانظر إلى نفسك ثم انتقل للعالم العلوي ثم السفلي. والنظر لغة: الإبصار والفكر. وعرفًا: ترتيب أمورٍ عدم
Clarifying the reality of speculative reasoning that leads to knowledge and that it is obligatory by revelation, contrary to the Mu'tazila. So look at yourself, then move to the upper world, then the lower. Speculative reasoning linguistically: sight and thought. Technically: the arrangement of matters... absence
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage begins a new section on the reality of speculative reasoning (nazar) that leads to knowledge of Allah. The author states that nazar is obligatory by revelation (shar'), contrary to the Mu'tazila who held it is obligatory by reason. Then the matn line 'fa-nẓur ilā nafsika...' is quoted as an imperative to engage in nazar by examining oneself and the cosmos. The definition of nazar is given: linguistically it means vision (ibṣār) and thought (fikr); technically it is the arrangement (tartīb) of matters (umūr) that leads to knowledge. The text cuts off, but the full definition typically continues with 'lead to knowledge.'

Definition and Obligation of al-Nazar
Defines nazar linguistically and technically, states obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic and technical definitions of nazar (speculative reasoning) and why it is obligatory.

Core Idea
Speculative reasoning is the method to move from the known to the unknown about Allah.

This lesson introduces the foundational concept of nazar, the intellectual method by which Muslims arrive at knowledge of Allah. The author begins by establishing its obligation and then defines it both linguistically and technically, providing a clear example of syllogistic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice how the author uses an imperative form to indicate obligation.
The passage begins with a statement that nazar is obligatory.
2
Identify the two definitions
Find the linguistic definition (language) and the technical definition (terminology). Note the difference between sight/thought and arrangement of known things.
3
Understand the example
The author gives a syllogism: 'The world is changing; everything changing is originated; therefore the world is originated.' Work through how this is an arrangement of known premises to reach an unknown conclusion.
The unknown here was the origination of the world before reasoning.
4
Reflect on the alternative definition
Shaykh al-Islam defines nazar more broadly as thought that leads to knowledge or belief. Compare this with the technical definition.
Both definitions emphasize the goal of reaching knowledge.

بيان حقيقة النظر الموصل إلى المعرفة فهو واجب، ولذا أتى بصيغة الأمر في قوله: فانظر إلى نفسك ثم انتقل للعالم العلوي ثم السفلي. (فانظر) أيها المكلف المخاطب. والنظر لغة: الإبصار والفكر. وعرفًا: ترتيب أمور معلومة ليتوصل بها - أي بترتيبها - إلى مجهول، أي: إلى علمه، كترتيب الصغرى مع الكبرى في قولنا: (العالم متغير وكل متغير حادث) فإنه موصل للعلم بحدوث العالم، المجهول قبل ذلك الترتيب. وعرفه شيخ الإسلام بأنه: فكر يؤدي إلى علم أو اعتقاد.
Exposition of the reality of speculative reasoning that leads to knowledge: it is obligatory, and therefore the imperative form was used in His statement: 'So look at yourself, then move to the upper world, then the lower.' (So look) O morally responsible addressed one. And nazar linguistically is: sight and thought. And technically: the arrangement of known things in order to reach through them—i.e., through their arrangement—an unknown thing, i.e., knowledge of it, like the arrangement of the minor premise with the major premise in our statement: 'The world is changing, and everything changing is originated'—for it leads to knowledge of the origination of the world, which was unknown before that arrangement. And Shaykh al-Islam defined it as: thought that leads to knowledge or belief.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'nazar' (speculative reasoning) that leads to knowledge and establishes its obligation. The author begins by stating that nazar is obligatory, citing the imperative form 'look' in the poem. He then gives the linguistic definition: nazar means sight (ibsar) and thought (fikr). The technical definition follows: it is the arrangement of known premises to arrive at an unknown conclusion. He illustrates with the syllogism: 'The world is changing; everything changing is originated; therefore the world is originated.' Finally, he reports the definition of Shaykh al-Islam, which is broader, defining it as thought that leads to knowledge or belief.

Speculative reasoning (nazar) is the deliberate arrangement of known premises to arrive at an unknown conclusion, and it is obligatory for knowing Allah.

Defining Nazar: The Path to Knowledge
The commentator defines nazar and distinguishes its two meanings, with a note on its linguistic range.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two technical definitions of nazar in kalam and how it functions to produce knowledge.

Core Idea
Nazar is the structured use of known premises to uncover unknown truths.

This lesson unpacks the foundational term nazar (speculative reasoning), which is the engine of all theological argument in kalam. The text defines it first as the arrangement of known premises to attain an unknown conclusion, then as thought that leads to knowledge or belief. Understanding these definitions is essential before engaging with any kalam proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation slowly. Notice the two definitions presented: one as 'tartib' (arrangement) and one as 'fikr' (thought).
Pay attention to the example of the syllogism about the world's change.
2
Identify the key distinction
Reflect on why nazar is defined in two ways. The first emphasizes logical arrangement, the second emphasizes the mental process. Consider how each captures a different aspect of reasoning.
The linguistic note about 'nazar' being homonymous between sight and thought may help.
3
Consider the schools' differences
The footnote discusses how correct nazar leads to knowledge: by rational necessity (al-Juwayni), by custom (al-Ash'ari), or by generation (Mu'tazila). Think about the implications for theological methodology.
This debate shows that even definitions carry theological weight.

ترتيب أمور معلومة (١) لِيُتَوصَّل بها - أي بترتيبها - إلى مجهول، أي: إلى علمه ، كترتيب الصُّغرى مع الكُبرى في قولنا: (العالم متغيّر (٢) وكلُّ متغيّر حادث) فإِنَّه مُوصل للعلم بحدوث العالم ، المجهول(۳) قبل ذلك الترتيب
وعرَّفه شيخ الاسلام بأنَّه : فِكْر يؤدي(٤) إلى علم أو اعتقادِ النفس في المحسوسات فيسمى تخيلاً. وعطف الفكر على الإبصار مغاير ، والنظر إن تعدى بـ (إلى) كان بمعنى الإبصار ، وإن تعدى بـ (في) كان بمعنى الفكر. وعلى ذلك: فالنظر مشترك بين الإبصار والفكر .
(١) قوله: (معلومة) أي حاضرة عند العقل تصوريا كان أو تصديقيا، يقينيا أو ظنيا أو جهلا، واشترط في الأمور المرتبة العلم.. لاستحالة التوصل بما ليس بحاصل بعد. قوله: (إلى مجهول) إنما اشترط في الأمر المطلوب الجهل .. لاستحالة تحصيل الحاصل. الشنواني.

والقصر، والبياض، والسواد، ونحو ذلك . و جواهر: أي أمور تقوم بأنفسها كذات زيد ، والجدار ، ونحوهما. وأنَّ المُتغير على الحقيقة الأعراض، وتغيرها ثابت بالمشاهدة . وأما الجواهر فإنَّها لا تتغيّر في أنفسها، وإِنَّما تتغيّر الأعراض الملازمة لها، فيكون تغيرها بتغير الأعراض الملازمة لها. محي.

والتعليل .
Clarification of the reality of nazar (speculative reasoning) that leads to knowledge: the arrangement of known things (1) to arrive by means of them—i.e., by arranging them—at an unknown thing, i.e., at knowledge of it, like the arrangement of the minor premise with the major premise in our statement: 'The world is changing (2) and everything changing is originated,' for it leads to knowledge of the world’s origination, the unknown (3) before that arrangement. And the Shaykh al-Islam defined it as: thought that leads (4) to knowledge or belief of the soul regarding sensible things, in which case it is called imagination. And the conjunction of thought with sight is in a different sense. Nazar, if it is transitive with 'ila' (to), has the meaning of sight; if transitive with 'fi' (in), it has the meaning of thought. Thus nazar is shared between sight and thought. (1) His saying: 'known things' means present in the mind, whether conceptual or assent, certain or probable or ignorant; he stipulated that the arranged things be known because it is impossible to reach something that is not yet obtained. His saying: 'to an unknown' he stipulated that the sought thing be unknown because it is impossible to obtain what is already obtained. al-Shanwani. (2) Know that the world is composed of accidents—i.e., things that subsist in others like motion, rest, length, shortness, whiteness, blackness, and the like—and substances: i.e., things that subsist in themselves like the essence of Zayd, the wall, and the like. And that changing in reality is [a property] of accidents, and their change is established by observation. As for substances, they do not change in themselves, but rather the accidents inseparable from them change, so their change is by the change of the accidents inseparable from them. Muhyi. (3) His saying: 'the unknown' is a description of 'origination'. (4) His saying: 'thought that leads'—this definition is more general than the previous one, and it belongs to the usuliyyun, i.e., specialists in usul al-din and usul al-fiqh. Taking 'thought' as a genus for nazar is based on their equivalence, which is the well-known view, contrary to those who claim it is more general and thus prevented interpreting nazar by it. By his saying 'thought that leads' he excludes thought that does not lead, like one who thinks without reaching the sought object, as in most inner speech; this is not called nazar. al-Shanwani. And that correct nazar leads to knowledge either by way of rational necessity, like the necessary concomitance between substance and accident, so that the existence of one without the other is rationally impossible and not subject to power; rather they either exist together or are both non-existent—this is the madhhab of Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni, which al-Amir and al-Shanwani preferred in their glosses. Or the concomitance is customary, admitting of exception—this is the madhhab of Shaykh al-Ash'ari. The Mu'tazila said by 'tawallud' (generation): that the act necessitates for its agent something else. The philosophers said by necessitation and causation.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage defines the technical term 'nazar' (speculative reasoning) and clarifies its meaning and scope. The commentator begins by stating that the passage is a clarification of the reality of nazar that leads to knowledge. He then gives the first definition: nazar is the arrangement of known things to arrive at an unknown. He illustrates this with the classic kalam syllogism: 'the world is changing, and everything changing is originated.' This syllogism leads to knowledge of the world's origination, which was unknown before the arrangement. The commentator adds a second definition from Shaykh al-Islam (likely al-Ash'ari or a later authority): nazar is thought that leads to knowledge or belief regarding sensible things; if it leads to belief about sensibles, it is called imagination. He then notes a linguistic point: the word 'nazar' is homonymous between sight (when transitive with 'ila') and thought (when transitive with 'fi'). This clarifies that nazar in the technical sense is the latter, but the word shares both meanings. The footnotes expand on the definitions. Footnote 1 explains that 'known things' (معلومة) means present in the mind, whether conceptual or assent, certain or probable, and that the unknown must not be already known. Footnote 2 explains that the world consists of accidents (changing) and substances (not changing in themselves). Footnote 3 is brief. Footnote 4 discusses the second definition: it is more general and applies to usul al-din and usul al-fiqh; it distinguishes nazar from mere thought that does not lead; it also presents a significant kalam discussion on how correct nazar leads to knowledge—either by rational necessity (the view of al-Juwayni) or by customary concomitance (the view of al-Ash'ari), with the Mu'tazila and philosophers having their own views. The commentator does not decisively choose one, but notes preferences.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think nazar is any random thought or reflection.
Correction: Nazar must be a structured arrangement of known premises that leads to a result; random or aimless thought does not qualify.

Checkpoint

B) The arrangement of known things to reach an unknown
C) Any thought that occurs to the mind
D) A type of belief

Key Takeaway
Nazar in kalam is not mere thought but the deliberate ordering of knowledge to uncover new truths, forming the core of rational theology.

The Linguistic Definition of Faith (Iman)
The author defines faith linguistically as affirmation and explains its root meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic definition of faith (iman) as affirmation (tasdiq) and its root meaning of security.

Core Idea
Faith linguistically means heartfelt affirmation, not mere verbal confession.

Before we can discuss the theological dimensions of faith, we must first understand what the word 'iman' means in the Arabic language. The author begins by grounding the discussion in the linguistic definition, which centers on affirmation of truth. He also notes that this definition contrasts with common misunderstandings that reduce faith to speech or actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the passage carefully, noting the key terms 'الإيمان', 'التصديق', and 'الأمن'. See how the author defines faith in relation to security.
2
Understand the linguistic definition
The author states that iman means tasdiq (affirmation), which is acquiescence and acceptance of a judgment as true. The root is 'amn' (security), implying that faith removes fear of rejection.
3
Distinguish linguistic from technical
Notice that after giving the linguistic definition, the author signals that the technical definition is debated. This lesson focuses on the linguistic meaning.

بيان حقيقة الإيمان
الإيمان في اللغة معناه : التصديق ، ومعنى التصديق : الإذعان للحكم وقبوله والاعتراف بكونه صادقا، والأصل فيه: (الأمن) الذي هو ضد الخوف ، وكأنَّ حقيقة قولنا: «آمن فلان» أنه نفى عنه خوف التكذيب والمخالفة .
