Three Scholarly Interpretations of the Sight (Basar) in the Vision of Allah
Author explains three ways the 'basar' (sight) is understood in ru'ya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three scholarly interpretations of how the sight (basar) is understood in the vision of Allah, and how transcendence is maintained.

Core Idea
The vision of Allah can be understood in three ways, all upheld by tanzih.

This lesson explores a key footnote from al-Shanwani that discusses the meaning of 'basar' in the context of ru'ya. The author presents three interpretations from the scholars, ranging from the physical eye to the whole face, while stressing that Allah is transcendent beyond any physical limitation. This variety reflects the depth of Islamic theological discourse.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to get the overall meaning.
Notice the three numbered ways.
2
Identify the three ways
List the three interpretations: 1) by the known sight (pupil), 2) by every part of the body, 3) by the whole face (based on Qur'an 75:22-24).
3
Consider the grammatical point
Note that the letter 'ba' in 'bi'l-basar' is for the proximate instrument (al-āla al-qarība). Understand how this affects interpretation.
This distinguishes between instrument and other meanings.
4
Reflect on tanzih
All three interpretations maintain tanzih (transcendence). Ask: Why is it important that Allah is not limited to physical sight?
Tanzih safeguards against anthropomorphism.
5
Address common misunderstandings
Some think seeing Allah requires a physical eye. The passage shows other possibilities like the whole face, all while preserving transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
محي . (۱) قوله: (بمعنى المحل) وهو النقطة التي في وسط العين المسمى بإنسانها ، ذكره المصنف في حاشيته ، وقضية هذا أنه تعالى لا يُرى إلا بالبصر المعلوم ، وهذه إحدى طرق ثلاث ، والطريقة الثانية: أنه تعالى يُرى بكل جزء من أجزاء البدن، والطريقة الثالثة: أنه يُرى بجميع الوجه ; لظاهر قوله تعالى: ﴿وُجُوهٌ يَوْمَئِذٍ نَاضِرَةٌ إِلَى رَبِّهَا نَاظِرَةٌ﴾ [القيامة : ٢٢ - ٢٤] وعلى كل فمع التنزيه، ولا مانع من اختلاف ذلك باختلاف الأشخاص، وهذا التفسير على أن الباء داخلة على الآلة القريبة . الشنواني . (۲) قوله : (أو القوة) معطوف على (المحل ) أي : بمعنى القوة المرتبة في العصبتين المجوفتين. الشنواني.
Mahi? (1) His saying: 'meaning the place' — and it is the point in the middle of the eye called its pupil, mentioned by the author in his marginalia. The implication of this is that He (exalted) is not seen except with the known sight. And this is one of three ways. The second way: that He is seen with every part of the body. The third way: that He is seen with the whole face, due to the apparent meaning of His saying: 'Faces on that day are radiant, looking at their Lord' (al-Qiyama 22-24). In all cases, with transcendence, and there is no impediment to its variation according to different persons. And this interpretation is on the basis that the 'ba' is for the proximate instrument. al-Shanwani. (2) His saying: 'or the power' is coordinated with 'the place', i.e., meaning the power placed in the two hollow nerves. al-Shanwani.
Source pages p. 373

Commentary
This footnote (from the commentator al-Shanwani) explains the interpretation of the word 'basar' (sight) in the context of the vision of Allah. It begins with a marginal note by the author (al-Mureed?) about the term 'al-mahall' (the place), which is the pupil of the eye. The first of three ways (ṭuruq thālath) is that Allah is seen only with the known sight (al-baṣar al-ma‘lūm) — i.e., the physical eye. The second way is that He is seen with every part of the body. The third way is that He is seen with the whole face, based on the apparent meaning of the verse 'Faces on that day are radiant, looking at their Lord.' The commentator stresses that in all interpretations, transcendence (tanzīh) is maintained — Allah is not like bodies, and there is no contradiction in these interpretations differing according to persons. The grammatical point is made that the particle 'ba' in 'bi'l-baṣar' is for the proximate instrument (āla qarība). The second part explains 'or the power' as referring to the optic nerve. This lesson thus presents the theological and linguistic nuances of how the 'sight' of Allah is conceptualized.

The First Testimony and the Three Rational Judgments
First testimony yields divine attributes and three rational judgments

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the testimony 'La ilaha illa Allah' entails the three rational judgments (wajib, mustahil, ja'iz) regarding Allah.

Core Idea
The first testimony logically necessitates God's attributes and the three rational judgments.

This lesson focuses on the first part of the passage, where the author unpacks the meaning of 'La ilaha illa Allah'. The testimony is not just a statement but a dense theological claim that implies necessary attributes of Allah, such as self-sufficiency and pre-eternality. From these attributes, the three categories of rational judgment—necessary, impossible, and permissible—are derived.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the first testimony
Read the Arabic and translation of the first part, focusing on how the author derives attributes from the testimony.
Notice the logical connections: 'وجوب الوجود' leads to 'القدم الذاتي' and then to 'استغناؤه'.
2
Identify the derived necessary attributes
List the attributes that the author says are necessitated by the first testimony, such as necessity of existence, pre-eternality, self-sufficiency, etc.
These attributes are all 'wajib' for Allah.
3
Understand the three rational judgments
The passage concludes that the first testimony includes the three rational judgments: wajib (necessary), mustahil (impossible), ja'iz (possible) regarding Allah. See how each is applied.
For example, attributes like life and power are wajib; their opposites (e.g., ignorance) are mustahil; contingent actions are ja'iz.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في بيان تضمُّنِ الشَّهادتين لجميع العقائد الإيمانية الوَاجِبة. ونفتها عن كلّ ما سواه، وحقيقة الألوهية: وجوب الوجودِ والقدم الذاتي، ويلزم منه استغناؤه عن كلّ ما سواه، وافتقار كلّ ما سواه إليه ، كما يوجب له البقاء، ومخالفة الممكنات، والقيام بالذات والتنزه عن النقائص كالأغراض الأفعال والأحكام، وعن وجوب شيء عليه تعالى ؛ لئلا يكون مُستكملا بفعله أو تركه ؛ فلا يثبت له الاستغناء المطلق، ووجوب افتقار الممكنات إليه يستلزم وجوبَ حياته ، وعموم قدرته وإرادته وعلمه ، ووحدته وعدم تأثير شيء سواه تعالى في شيء منها، ومتى وجبت هذه الأمور له تعالى ؛ استحالت نقائضها عليه تعالى، وجاز ما سوى ذلك في حقه ؛ فقد اشتملت الجملة الأولى على أقسام الحكم العقلىّ الثَّلاثة الراجعة إليه تعالى. ويؤخذ مِن الجملة الثَّانية : وجوب الإيمان بسائر الأنبياء والرسل والملائكة والكتب السَّماويّة واليوم الآخر وما فيه ؛ إذ التصريح برسالته يستلزم تصديقه في كلّ ما جاء به، ومن جملته ما ذُكر ، ويُعلم منه أيضا: وجوب صدقهم، واستحالة الخيانة والكذب عليهم، وجواز جميع الأعراض البشرية التي لا تُنقِصُ مراتبهم عليهم الصَّلاة والسلام، وهذه جملة أقسام الحكم العقلي المُتعلّقة بالرُّسل عليهم الصَّلاة والسَّلام
In explaining the encompassment of the two testimonies for all obligatory faith beliefs: and their negation of everything else, and the reality of divinity: necessity of existence and essential pre-eternality, and from it follows His self-sufficiency from everything else, and the need of everything else for Him, as it necessitates for Him permanence, and opposition to contingent things, and self-subsistence, and transcendence above deficiencies such as purposes, actions, and judgments, and above the obligation of anything upon Him the Exalted; so that He not be completed by His action or abandonment; thus absolute self-sufficiency is not established for Him, and the necessity of contingent things' need for Him necessitates the necessity of His life, and universality of His power, will, and knowledge, and His unity and the lack of effect of anything other than Him the Exalted in any of them. And when these matters are necessary for Him the Exalted, their opposites are impossible for Him the Exalted, and what is other than that is permissible regarding Him; thus the first statement has included the three divisions of rational judgment pertaining to Him the Exalted. And from the second statement is taken: the obligation of belief in all the prophets, messengers, angels, heavenly books, and the Last Day and what is in it; since the declaration of his message entails affirming him in everything he brought, and among it is what was mentioned. And from it is also known: the necessity of their truthfulness, the impossibility of treachery and lying upon them, and the permissibility of all human accidents that do not diminish their ranks, peace and blessings be upon them. And this is the totality of the divisions of rational judgment pertaining to the messengers, peace and blessings be upon them.
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage defines how the two testimonies (shahadatayn: 'La ilaha illa Allah' and 'Muhammadun Rasul Allah') encompass all obligatory beliefs. The author first explains that the first testimony, 'there is no god but Allah', implies the reality of divinity: necessary existence, pre-eternality, self-sufficiency, and that all else depends on Him. From this necessity come other necessary attributes: permanence, distinctness from contingents, self-subsistence, transcendence above deficiencies like purposes and actions, and that nothing is obligatory upon Him. The author then states that these necessary attributes entail the impossibility of their opposites and permissibility of what is not necessary. Thus the first testimony yields the three rational judgments (wajib, mustahil, ja'iz) regarding God. The second testimony, 'Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah', requires belief in everything he brought: faith in all prophets, messengers, angels, scriptures, and the Last Day. It also necessitates that prophets are necessarily truthful, it is impossible for them to betray or lie, and permissible for them to have human accidents that do not diminish their rank. This gives the three rational judgments regarding prophets.

Correction: The passage shows it entails necessary attributes of Allah and the three rational judgments.

Key Takeaway

When you recite 'La ilaha illa Allah', do you truly feel the weight of Allah's absolute self-sufficiency, or do you sometimes act as if He needs your worship? How would your daily life change if you internalized that He is not completed by your deeds?
?
The passage says that Allah transcends being obligated to do anything. Have you ever felt entitled to a reward from Allah, as if your actions obligate Him? What would it look like to worship Him purely out of need, not out of a sense of deserving?
?
If Allah's life, power, and will are necessary, and nothing else has effect, why do you still fear the opinions of people more than His displeasure? Write one concrete step to align your heart with this truth today.

The Second Testimony and Belief in Prophethood
Second testimony yields belief in prophethood and three rational judgments

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the testimony 'Muhammadun Rasul Allah' entails the three rational judgments (wajib, mustahil, ja'iz) regarding prophets.

Core Idea
The second testimony requires belief in all prophetic teachings and implies their necessary truthfulness and impossibility of lying.

The second part of the passage shifts to the testimony 'Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah'. The author shows that this declaration logically necessitates faith in all that the Prophet brought, including other prophets, angels, scriptures, and the Last Day. From this, the three rational judgments regarding prophets are derived: it is necessary for them to be truthful, impossible for them to betray or lie, and permissible for them to have human accidents that do not detract from their rank.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the second testimony
Read the Arabic and translation of the second part, focusing on how the author deduces obligations and judgments from the testimony.
Notice the phrase 'يؤخذ مِن الجملة الثَّانية' — 'from the second statement is taken'.
2
Identify what belief is obligated
List the things we must believe in according to the second testimony: all prophets, messengers, angels, books, the Last Day, etc.
This flows from affirming the Prophet's message.
3
Understand the three rational judgments for prophets
The passage states it is wajib for prophets to be truthful, mustahil for them to lie or betray, and ja'iz for them to have human accidents that don't diminish rank.
This mirrors the structure of the three judgments for Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في بيان تضمُّنِ الشَّهادتين لجميع العقائد الإيمانية الوَاجِبة. ونفتها عن كلّ ما سواه، وحقيقة الألوهية: وجوب الوجودِ والقدم الذاتي، ويلزم منه استغناؤه عن كلّ ما سواه، وافتقار كلّ ما سواه إليه ، كما يوجب له البقاء، ومخالفة الممكنات، والقيام بالذات والتنزه عن النقائص كالأغراض الأفعال والأحكام، وعن وجوب شيء عليه تعالى ؛ لئلا يكون مُستكملا بفعله أو تركه ؛ فلا يثبت له الاستغناء المطلق، ووجوب افتقار الممكنات إليه يستلزم وجوبَ حياته ، وعموم قدرته وإرادته وعلمه ، ووحدته وعدم تأثير شيء سواه تعالى في شيء منها، ومتى وجبت هذه الأمور له تعالى ؛ استحالت نقائضها عليه تعالى، وجاز ما سوى ذلك في حقه ؛ فقد اشتملت الجملة الأولى على أقسام الحكم العقلىّ الثَّلاثة الراجعة إليه تعالى. ويؤخذ مِن الجملة الثَّانية : وجوب الإيمان بسائر الأنبياء والرسل والملائكة والكتب السَّماويّة واليوم الآخر وما فيه ؛ إذ التصريح برسالته يستلزم تصديقه في كلّ ما جاء به، ومن جملته ما ذُكر ، ويُعلم منه أيضا: وجوب صدقهم، واستحالة الخيانة والكذب عليهم، وجواز جميع الأعراض البشرية التي لا تُنقِصُ مراتبهم عليهم الصَّلاة والسلام، وهذه جملة أقسام الحكم العقلي المُتعلّقة بالرُّسل عليهم الصَّلاة والسَّلام
In explaining the encompassment of the two testimonies for all obligatory faith beliefs: and their negation of everything else, and the reality of divinity: necessity of existence and essential pre-eternality, and from it follows His self-sufficiency from everything else, and the need of everything else for Him, as it necessitates for Him permanence, and opposition to contingent things, and self-subsistence, and transcendence above deficiencies such as purposes, actions, and judgments, and above the obligation of anything upon Him the Exalted; so that He not be completed by His action or abandonment; thus absolute self-sufficiency is not established for Him, and the necessity of contingent things' need for Him necessitates the necessity of His life, and universality of His power, will, and knowledge, and His unity and the lack of effect of anything other than Him the Exalted in any of them. And when these matters are necessary for Him the Exalted, their opposites are impossible for Him the Exalted, and what is other than that is permissible regarding Him; thus the first statement has included the three divisions of rational judgment pertaining to Him the Exalted. And from the second statement is taken: the obligation of belief in all the prophets, messengers, angels, heavenly books, and the Last Day and what is in it; since the declaration of his message entails affirming him in everything he brought, and among it is what was mentioned. And from it is also known: the necessity of their truthfulness, the impossibility of treachery and lying upon them, and the permissibility of all human accidents that do not diminish their ranks, peace and blessings be upon them. And this is the totality of the divisions of rational judgment pertaining to the messengers, peace and blessings be upon them.
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage defines how the two testimonies (shahadatayn: 'La ilaha illa Allah' and 'Muhammadun Rasul Allah') encompass all obligatory beliefs. The author first explains that the first testimony, 'there is no god but Allah', implies the reality of divinity: necessary existence, pre-eternality, self-sufficiency, and that all else depends on Him. From this necessity come other necessary attributes: permanence, distinctness from contingents, self-subsistence, transcendence above deficiencies like purposes and actions, and that nothing is obligatory upon Him. The author then states that these necessary attributes entail the impossibility of their opposites and permissibility of what is not necessary. Thus the first testimony yields the three rational judgments (wajib, mustahil, ja'iz) regarding God. The second testimony, 'Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah', requires belief in everything he brought: faith in all prophets, messengers, angels, scriptures, and the Last Day. It also necessitates that prophets are necessarily truthful, it is impossible for them to betray or lie, and permissible for them to have human accidents that do not diminish their rank. This gives the three rational judgments regarding prophets.

Key Terms
الشهادتان
الحكم العقلي
الأعراض البشرية
الرسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Believing that prophets are infallible in all aspects, including human accidents like illness.
Correction: The passage allows human accidents that do not diminish their rank, so they can experience normal human things without sin.

Checkpoint
What is the rational judgment regarding the truthfulness of prophets according to the second testimony?
A) Wajib (necessary)
B) Mustahil (impossible)
C) Ja'iz (permissible)
D) Makruh (disliked)

Key Takeaway
The second testimony yields the three rational judgments for prophets: truthfulness is necessary, lying is impossible, and harmless human traits are permissible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The passage teaches that it is impossible for prophets to lie. Reflect: In your daily life, do you treat the authentic hadith with the same certainty as the Quran? Or do you allow cultural preferences to cast doubt on some teachings? What would change if you truly believed every word from the Prophet is truthful?
?
Prophets had human traits like eating and sleeping. Does this human dimension make you feel more connected to them, or does it diminish their status in your eyes? How can you maintain reverent love while remembering their humanity without falling into excess or neglect?

Defining Shar': Linguistic and Technical Meanings
The author defines the term shar' literally and technically, with a critical note on the definition's scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic and terminological definitions of the term shar' (Sharia) and the scholarly critique that refines it.

Core Idea
Shar' literally means clarification, but technically it means making things permissible or forbidden.

In this lesson, we dive into the foundational term 'shar'' (Sharia). The author first gives its literal meaning, then its technical definition in Islamic legal theory. However, the commentator notes that the technical definition as given is incomplete, and we will see how scholars address that deficiency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on shar'
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the progression from literal to technical meaning.
2
Identify the related terms
The passage defines three related terms: shāri' (Lawgiver), sharī'ah (path), and mashrū' (legislated). Understand how they are derived from the root.
3
Examine the critique
The commentator points out that the definition only mentions permissible and forbidden, leaving out obligation, recommendation, and dislike. Consider the two proposed refinements.
This critique shows the care scholars take in defining terms precisely.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والشَّرْعُ لغةً: البيانُ، واصطلاحًا تجويز الشيء أو تحريمه، أي: جعله جائزا أو حراماً. والشَّارِعُ : مُبيِّنُ الأحكام، والشَّريعة: الطَّريقة في الدين. والمَشرُوع: ما أظهره الشَّرع. قوله: (تجويز الشيء) في العبارة قصور، وكان الأولى أن يزيد الكراهة والوجوب والندب، فالشرع عبارة عن الأحكام الشرعية، إلا أن يُقال: المراد بالتجويز ما قابل التحريم، فيشمل الإيجاب والندب والكراهة والإباحة، والأحسنُ أن يراد به النّسب التامة، كثبوت الوجوب للصلاة.
And al-shar' linguistically: the clarification, and terminologically: the making permissible of something or the prohibiting of it, i.e., making it permissible or forbidden. And al-shāri' (the Lawgiver) is the one who clarifies rulings. And al-sharī'ah is the path in religion. And al-mashrū' (the legislated) is what the shar' has manifested. His statement: (the making permissible of something) - in the expression there is deficiency, and it would have been better to add dislike, obligation, and recommendation, for al-shar' is an expression for the legal rulings, unless it is said that what is meant by 'making permissible' is what is opposite of prohibition, so it includes obligation, recommendation, dislike, and permissibility. And the best is that what is intended by it is the complete relationships, such as the establishment of obligation for prayer.
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
This passage defines the term shar' linguistically and terminologically. It begins by stating that linguistically shar' means 'clarification (al-bayān)'. Terminologically, it is defined as 'making something permissible (tajwīz) or prohibiting it (taḥrīm)', meaning to declare it permissible or forbidden. The passage then derives related terms: al-shāri' is the one who clarifies rulings (primarily Allah, but used metaphorically for the Prophet), al-sharī'ah is the path in religion, and al-mashrū' is what the shar' has manifested. A commentary note then points out a deficiency in the definition: it only mentions permissible and forbidden, leaving out obligatory, recommended, and disliked. It suggests an improvement: either take 'tajwīz' to mean what is opposite of prohibition (thus including obligation, recommendation, dislike, and permissibility) or better, interpret it as the complete relationships (e.g., obligation for prayer). This shows the care taken to refine the definition.

Key Terms
شرع
شارع
شريعة
مشروع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The technical definition of shar' as 'making permissible or prohibited' is complete and covers all legal rulings.
Correction: The definition is deficient because it omits obligation, recommendation, and dislike; scholars refine it to include all five categories or interpret 'tajwīz' more broadly.

Checkpoint
What is the terminological definition of shar' according to the passage?
A) It is the path in religion.
B) It is the making permissible or prohibiting of something.
C) It is the clarification of rulings.
D) It is the legislated rulings.

Key Takeaway
The term shar' is defined both literally (clarification) and technically (making permissible or forbidden), but the technical definition requires refinement to encompass all legal rulings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What would change in your daily practice if you truly believed each ruling is Allah's direct guidance for your life? Write down one command you have been neglecting and commit to acting on it this week.
?
The author shows that the definition of shar‘ must include all five categories of rulings—not just what is permissible or forbidden. Reflect: Are you sometimes selective in following only the clear prohibitions while ignoring the recommended (mandūb) or disliked (makrūh) acts? Think of one hated deed you continue doing even though it is disliked, and one recommended deed you avoid out of laziness. What is the state of your heart that allows this inconsistency, and what step will you take today to align with the full spectrum of Allah’s law?
?
Imagine standing before Allah on the Day of Judgment and being asked about the very definition of His law: did you seek its clarification (bayān) or did you rely on your own assumptions? When you are uncertain about a ruling, do you actively seek knowledge from reliable sources, or do you remain in ignorance? The Doctrine of Abrogation (Naskh) in the Quran
Definition and defense of naskh in the Quran.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of naskh, its rational permissibility, and the refutation of objections against abrogating Quranic verses.

Core Idea
Abrogation is a rational possibility that does not compromise the Quran's integrity.

This lesson establishes the concept of naskh (abrogation) as understood by Ahl al-Sunnah. It defines the term, clarifies its scope, and addresses key objections raised against applying abrogation to the Quran, particularly the challenge from the verse's definitiveness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Pay attention to the numbered footnotes and the main text flow.
2
Identify the definition and key components
Find the linguistic and terminological definition of naskh, and note the doctrine of Ahl al-Haqq regarding its occurrence and permissibility.
The definition includes both what has occurred and what is possible.
3
Understand the difference between permissibility and occurrence
Note that the passage distinguishes between what is rationally possible (جواز عقلي) and what actually happened (وقوع). The obligation to know Allah is included in the scope of possible abrogation, but it did not occur.
This clarifies that abrogation is a rational possibility, not a denial of obligations.
4
Examine the objections and their refutations
The passage presents two objections to abrogation of the Quran by solitary reports, along with their answers. The first is based on the definitiveness of the Quran's text, the second on the verse about falsehood not approaching it.
The refutations show that abrogation does not compromise the Quran's authenticity.
5
Note the conclusion about abrogation by solitary reports
The passage concludes that there is no impediment to abrogating the Quran by solitary reports, as long as the definitive nature of the text is distinguished from its indication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعريف النسخ لغة واصطلاحا (۲) ، وهو هو مذهب أهل الحقِّ (۱)، ومفهومه عدم وقوع نَسخ الجميع الصحيح إجماعاً ، وإن كان كلُّ حكم شرعي قابلا للنسخ كلا أو بعضا على المُختار ، وشَملَ البعض (۳) القرآني أيضا (٤) ، خلافا لمن منعه (ه) كأبي مسلم الأصفهاني (٦) = وَمَا فِي ذَا لَهُ مِنْ غَضَ) أي: وليسَ في هذا الحكم العام ـ وهو تجويز والكفر حرام، ويجوز جوازا عقليا نسخُ ذلك ، بأن تُحرَّم المعرفة ويجبَ الكُفْرُ ، ولكن لم يقع ، وهذا الشمول يقتضي أن الجواز في قول المصنف المراد منه الجواز العقلي ، وقد علمت ما فيه . الشنواني. (۲) قوله: (ومفهومه عدم وقوع نسخ الجميع إن قلت كلام المُصنف في الجواز، قلنا: كأَنَّ الشارحَ جعلَ كلا من الجواز والوقوع ملتفتا له فقوله أولا : (يشمل وجوب معرفته تعالى) ، التفتَ فيه للجواز، وقوله: (ومفهومه عدم ... الخ) التفتَ فيه للوقوع، وعليه يظهر ذكر (البعض) في كلام المصنف، فليتأمل. الأمير والشنواني. الشنواني . (۳) قوله : ( وشمل البعض) أي: الجائز نسخه. (٤) ووقع في المطبوع: (وشَمِلَ البعض القرآن أيضا). (ه) تمسك المانع بأنَّ القرآن قطعي فلا يُنسخُ بآحادٍ وأجيب بأنَّ القطعي متنه لا دلالته ، لكن أنت خبير بأنَّ الدلالة قد تكون قطعيةً كآية الاستقبال، فالحق أن يُقال: لا مانع من نسخه بالآحاد ، وتمسك المانع أيضا بقوله تعالى: ﴿وَلَا يَأْتِيهِ الْبَطِلُ مِنْ بَيْنِ يَدَيْهِ وَلَا مِنْ خَلْفِهِ فلو نسخ بعضه لتطرق إليه البطلان ، وأجيب بأنَّ الضمير لمجموع القرآن ، وهو لا ينسخ إجماعاً، ولأنَّ النسخ إبطال أو رفع للحكم لا باطل ، فإن الباطل ضد الحق. الأمير والشنواني. (٦) هو محمد بن بحر ، أبو مسلم الأصفهاني من كبار أئمة أهل البدعة المعتزلي ، كان نحويا كاتبا بليغا متكلما ، عالما بالتفسير ، من مؤلفاته: ناسخ الحديث و منسوخه توفي سنة : (۳۲۲هـ) وهو ليس الجاحظ كما توهمه بعضهم.
The definition of abrogation (naskh) linguistically and terminologically (2). And it is the doctrine of the People of Truth (1), and its meaning is the non-occurrence of abrogation of the whole, which is correct by consensus, even though every legal ruling is subject to abrogation in whole or in part according to the chosen view. And it includes (3) the Quranic part also (4), contrary to those who forbid it, such as Abu Muslim al-Isfahani (6). And what is in this general ruling? (i.e., there is no objection) (i.e., there is no flaw in this general ruling, which is the permissibility of abrogation) and unbelief is forbidden, and it is permissible by rational permissibility to abrogate that, by making knowledge forbidden and unbelief obligatory, but it did not occur. And this inclusion requires that the permissibility in the author's statement is meant as rational permissibility, and you have come to know what is in it. Al-Shanwani. (1) His statement: 'as it is the doctrine of the People of Truth' — its opposite is that unbelief is rationally evil, and the obligation to know Allah, Exalted, is rationally good, so it is not valid to abrogate them, but this is incorrect because goodness is what the Law makes good, and evil is what the Law makes evil. Al-Amir and al-Shanwani. (2) His statement: 'and its meaning is the non-occurrence of abrogation of the whole' — if you say: the author's speech is about permissibility, we say: it is as if the commentator considered both permissibility and occurrence as intended, so his first statement 'it includes the obligation to know Him, Exalted' — he attended to permissibility, and his statement 'and its meaning is the non-occurrence... etc.' — he attended to occurrence. And on this basis, the mention of 'some' in the author's words becomes clear, so reflect. Al-Amir and al-Shanwani. Al-Shanwani. (3) His statement: 'and it includes the part' — i.e., what is permissible to abrogate. (4) And in the printed version: 'and it includes the Quranic part also.' (5) The objector held that the Quran is definitive, so it cannot be abrogated by solitary reports. The response is that definitiveness is of its text, not its indication. But you are aware that the indication may be definitive, like the verse of the qibla. So the truth is to say: there is no impediment to abrogating it by solitary reports. The objector also held by His statement, Exalted: 'Falsehood cannot approach it from before or behind it' — so if some of it were abrogated, falsehood would enter upon it. The response is that the pronoun refers to the whole Quran, and it is not abrogated by consensus, and because abrogation is an annulment or removal of a ruling, not falsehood, for falsehood is the opposite of truth. Al-Amir and al-Shanwani. (6) He is Muhammad ibn Bahr, Abu Muslim al-Isfahani, one of the great imams of the people of innovation, the Mu'tazilite. He was a grammarian, eloquent writer, theologian, and expert in exegesis, among his works: 'The Abrogator and Abrogated of Hadith.' He died in 322 AH. He is not al-Jahiz, as some mistakenly thought.
Source pages p. 428

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'abrogation' (naskh) both linguistically and terminologically, and establishes it as the doctrine of Ahl al-Haqq (the People of Truth). The definition emphasizes that abrogation of the entire Sharia has not occurred, by consensus, even though every legal ruling is potentially abrogatable in part or whole according to the preferred opinion. Importantly, this includes the Quranic text itself, contrary to those who deny it, such as Abu Muslim al-Isfahani. The commentator then addresses a nuance: the general ruling that abrogation is permissible does not mean it actually happened for the most fundamental obligations like knowing Allah and the prohibition of disbelief; rather, it is only a rational possibility. The inclusion of even these fundamental rulings in the scope of potential abrogation indicates that the author's 'permissibility' refers to rational permissibility (جواز عقلي), not actual occurrence. The footnotes elaborate further: the first footnote defends the Ash'ari position that good and evil are determined by revelation, not reason. The second footnote reconciles the commentator's statements, showing that he attended to both permissibility and occurrence. The third and fourth footnotes clarify that Quranic verses can be abrogated by other verses or even by solitary hadith, refuting objections based on the definitiveness of the Quran and the verse about falsehood not approaching it. The final footnote provides a brief biography of Abu Muslim al-Isfahani, distinguishing him from al-Jahiz.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that abrogating Quranic verses by solitary hadith denies the Quran's definitiveness or introduces falsehood.
Correction: The passage clarifies that definitiveness is of the text, not its indication, and that abrogation is removal of a ruling, not falsehood, so the Quran remains protected.

Checkpoint

A) Because it is impossible for Allah to abrogate such obligations

C) Because the obligation to know Allah is not a legal ruling
D) Because the commentator explicitly states it is rational

Key Takeaway
Abrogation is a rationally permissible concept that applies to the Quran, and objections based on definitiveness or falsehood are refuted by scholarly consensus and proper interpretation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage distinguishes between what is rationally possible (جواز عقلي) and what actually occurred (وقوع). Are there areas in your life where you confuse possibility with reality—worrying about what could happen instead of trusting what has actually been decreed? How can you train your heart to rest in the actual rather than the possible?
?
Abu Muslim al-Isfahani was a brilliant scholar, yet he erred on the issue of abrogation. Do you have the humility to admit that even the most learned people can be wrong on matters of religion, and are you willing to subject your own understanding to the consensus of Ahl al-Sunnah, even when it contradicts your reasoning?

The Quran in Usul and Kalam
Defines the Quran in usul and kalam, declares it the best miracle

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two distinct definitions of the Quran in usul al-fiqh and kalam, and why the Quran is considered the greatest and most enduring miracle.

Core Idea
The Quran is both a revealed composition and an eternal divine attribute.

This passage introduces the Quran as a central miracle of prophethood. It provides two complementary definitions: one from legal theory focusing on the revealed text, and another from theology focusing on the unspoken attribute of Allah. Understanding these definitions is key to grasping the Quran's unique status.

Guided Path
1
Contextualize the passage
The passage is part of a section on prophethood discussing miracles. Note the mention of countless miracles and the term 'غرر' (clear, famous).
This sets the stage for focusing on the Quran.
2
Read the first definition
In usul al-fiqh, the Quran is defined as 'النظم المنزل'—the revealed composition, recited as worship, challenging with its shortest sura.
This definition emphasizes the physical text and its role in worship.
3
Read the second definition
In kalam, the Quran is 'المعنى النفسي'—the internal meaning subsisting in Allah's essence, indicated by the revealed composition.
This definition focuses on the eternal attribute of speech.
4
Appreciate the conclusion
The Quran is declared the best and most enduring of the Prophet's miracles. Reflect on why this might be.

قسم النبوات وغير ذلك مما لا يحصى؛ ولذا وصفها بالكثرة المطلقة عن التقييد بعدد معين أو مبهم إيماء للعجز عن الإحاطة بها، وقوله: (غرر)، أي: واضحات مشهورات (منها كلام الله) تعالى المسمى في عرف الأصوليين بالقرآن، وهو: النظم المنزل عليه، المتعبد بتلاوته، المتحدي بأقصر سورة منه للإعجاز، وأما في عرف المتكلمين، فالمسمى به: المعنى النفسي القائم به: بذاته تعالى، المدلول للنظم المنزل، وهو أفضل معجزاته وأدومها

And other countless [miracles]; so he described them as numerous; and so he described them as absolute multitude without restriction to a specific or vague number, indicating inability to encompass them. Source pages p. 433

Commentary
This passage defines the Quran according to two disciplines and highlights its status as the greatest miracle. It begins by explaining the context of the section on prophethood, where the author describes the miracles as countless and uses the term 'غرر' (clear, famous). Then it specifies that among these miracles is the Speech of Allah, i.e., the Quran. The author then provides two definitions: (1) In the terminology of usul al-fiqh, it is the revealed composition (النظم المنزل) that is recited as worship and challenges with its shortest sura. (2) In the terminology of kalam (theology), it is the internal meaning (المعنى النفسي) that subsists in Allah's essence and is indicated by the revealed composition. Finally, it states that the Quran is the best and most enduring of the Prophet's miracles.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Believing the Quran is only a created physical text and not uncreated speech of Allah.
Correction: The passage distinguishes two definitions: the Quran as revealed composition (which is created in terms of letters and sounds) and as the internal meaning (which is an uncreated attribute of Allah). Both are valid in different disciplines.

The Ruling on the Soul and an Objection Answered
Sanusi clarifies the permissibility of discussing the soul and refutes an objection against its indivisibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the prohibition on discussing the soul's reality is one of disapproval (tanzih) rather than absolute forbiddance, and how the soul's subtlety refutes the objection that cutting a limb cuts the soul.

Core Idea
The soul's subtlety preserves its indivisibility despite bodily cuts.

This passage from Sanusi addresses a common misunderstanding about discussing the soul. Some think it is forbidden, but Sanusi clarifies that great scholars engaged in it, showing the prohibition is only one of disapproval. He then faces an objection about the soul being cut when a limb is severed, and answers with the soul's subtle nature.

Guided Path
1
Understand the ruling
Read the first part of the passage which states that the prohibition is for tanzih (disapproval), not tahrim (forbidden). Note the evidence: the Maliki scholars themselves discussed the soul's reality.
The term 'tanzih' is key – it means disapproval, not absolute prohibition.
2
Examine the objection
The passage presents an objection: if you cut a limb, the corresponding part of the soul would be cut, implying divisibility. This would contradict the belief that the soul remains intact after death.
3
Grasp the answer
The answer hinges on the soul's subtlety (latafah). It either retracts instantly before the cut or reunites immediately after, so no part of the soul is actually cut.
This preserves the soul's indivisibility.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this argument defends the soul's unity and why the subtlety explanation is necessary to counter materialistic objections.
This connects to broader discussions in kalam about the soul and body.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قسم السمعيات وإذا علمتَ النقل عن أهل السنة بالخوض في حقيقته فحسبك أي يكفيك في أن النهي للتنزيه خوض أهل مذهب مالك فيها فإنه ورد النص عنهم بهذا السند هو الطريق الموصلة إلى المتن استعمل هنا المسند أي فلو كان الخوض فيها ممتنعا لم يقدم عليه مثل هؤلاء الأكابر. وما أورد عليه من أنه إذا قطع عضو حيوان لزم قطع نظيره من الروح فلا يصح إطلاق القول ببقائها. يجاب عنه بأن لطافتها تقتضي سرعة انجذابها من ذلك العضو المقطوع قبل انفصاله أو سرعة الالتحام بعد القطع كما أن اللطافة مقتضية لانضمامه عند قطع عضو الجسد إلى باقي أجزاء الروح.
Section of Sam‘iyyat. And if you know the transmission from Ahl al-Sunnah concerning delving into His reality, then it suffices you, i.e., it suffices you that the prohibition is for tanzih (disapproval), the delving of the people of the school of Malik into it, for indeed the text came from them with this chain – it is the path leading to the text, here ‘musnad’ was used – i.e., if delving into it were forbidden, such great ones would not have undertaken it. And what was objected against it: that if a limb of an animal is cut, then cutting its counterpart from the soul necessarily follows, so the absolute statement of its remaining is not valid. It is answered about this: by the fact that its subtlety necessitates its rapid retraction from that severed limb before its separation, or rapid reunion after the cut, just as subtlety necessitates its gathering when a limb of the body is cut to the rest of the soul’s parts.
Source pages p. 494

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal argument defending the permissibility of discussing the reality of the soul (ruh) and refuting an objection. It begins by stating that the prohibition against such discussion is only for tanzih (disapproval, not absolute forbiddance), as evidenced by the fact that scholars of the Maliki school themselves engaged in it. The author then presents an objection: if one cuts a limb of a living being, the corresponding part of the soul would also be cut, implying divisibility and thus contradicting the belief that the soul persists undivided after death. The answer is that the soul's extreme subtlety (lutf) causes it to either retract instantly from the limb before the cut, or reunite immediately after the cut, just as the subtlety of the soul causes its parts to gather together when a body part is severed. Thus, no part of the soul is actually cut, preserving its indivisible nature.

Key Terms
الروح (al-ruh)
التنزيه (tanzih)
اللطافة (al-latafah)
السَّمْعِيَّات (sam‘iyyat)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the prohibition on discussing the soul is absolute (tahrim) and cannot be engaged in at all.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the prohibition is only for tanzih (disapproval), as evidenced by the fact that great Maliki scholars themselves discussed the soul's reality.

Checkpoint
What is the main argument used to refute the objection that cutting a limb cuts the soul?
A) The soul is not affected by bodily changes.
B) The soul is not located in the body at all.
C) The soul's subtlety causes it to retract or reunite rapidly, so no part is cut.
D) The objection is based on a false premise about the body.

Key Takeaway
The soul's extreme subtlety ensures it is not divided when limbs are severed, preserving its indivisible nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Your soul is stretched between earthly distractions and spiritual duties—how is it dividing you? What one practice will you adopt this week to gather your soul back to its center?

The Sunni Doctrine: Intellect as an Accident
Commentator states Sunni consensus on accidentally of intellect and provides definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni position that intellect is an accident ('arad) and that most scholars consider it from the category of sciences, contrasting with other groups.

Core Idea
Intellect is an accident, not a substance, according to Ahl al-Sunna.

The commentator begins by clarifying the nature of intellect, stating that the Sunni consensus is that it is an accident, not a substance. This lesson examines that consensus and the definitions from key authorities like al-Shafi'i and al-Ghazali.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the Sunni consensus
Start with the line 'وأقوال أهل السُّنَّة مُتطابقةٌ على عَرَضيَّتِه' and the surrounding commentary. Notice the agreement on accidentally.
The word 'عَرَضيَّتِه' means its being an accident.
2
Understand the contrast with other groups
The text mentions philosophers, Mu'tazila, and Khawarij who say intellect is a substance. Compare their view.
3
Examine al-Shafi'i's definition
Al-Shafi'i defined intellect as 'the instrument of discrimination' (آلة التمييز). Consider why this definition is neutral on substance/accident.
4
Reflect on al-Ghazali's view
Al-Ghazali describes a 'lordly subtlety' with three aspects. Note how this relates to the intellect being an accident.

The reality of the intellect. (Then look) in the books of the people (what they have explained) i.e., the interpretations and realities which they have clarified; because they are the subject matter for it, not in this poem, due to its small size. And the statements of Ahl al-Sunna are in agreement upon its being an accident, and most of them are that it is from the category of sciences. (1) His saying: (because they are the subject matter for it) is a cause for his saying: look in the books of the people. (2) In some copies: 'in this introduction'. (3) His saying: (upon its being an accident) i.e., that it is an accident, not a substance. Its opposite are the philosophers, who say it is a substance, and likewise the Mu'tazila and Khawarij. And some of them explained it as a substance by which the unseen things are perceived through intermediaries and the sensible things through observation, and some of them explained it otherwise. And al-Shafi'i explained it as the instrument of discrimination, and his explanation admits of both substantiality and accidentally. And in the words of al-Ghazali is what confirms that it is an abstract substance, and its summary is that there is a lordly subtlety known only to Allah, which in terms of its thinking is 'aql, in terms of the life of the body by it is life, and in terms of its desire and expression of it is soul, and the three are one in essence but different in consideration. And it is not said that every possessor of spirit necessarily has intellect, because the spirit is not intellect by itself, but by consideration of thinking. And know that the intellect is of five types: first: instinctive, which is in every human, believer and disbeliever; second: acquired, which is what a person acquires from associating with intelligent people, and it belongs to the believer and disbeliever as well; third: bestowed, which is the intellect of the believer by which he is guided to faith; fourth: the intellect of the ascetics, by which asceticism is attained; fifth: honorable, which is the intellect of our Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him), because it is the noblest of intellects. And knowledge is better than intellect because it is one of His attributes, and what is narrated about the virtue of intellect is fabricated with no basis, as stated by al-Jalal al-Suyuti. al-Shanawani. (4) In one copy: 'and the most important of them' which is wrong. So the meaning is that most of the sayings of Ahl al-Sunna are that intellect is from the category of sciences. · (5) His saying: (and most of them are that it is from the category of sciences) i.e., those who say it is an accident differed: some said it is from the category of sciences, and defined it as knowledge of some necessary sciences, which is the knowledge of the necessity of obligatories, the impossibility of impossibilities, and the possibility of possibilities, i.e., affirmation of them. Imam al-Haramayn and a group said: intellect is not a substance, because substances have attributes established for them and not established for others, and no name is derived from them for others. But intellect is an attribute established for the person, and the term 'intelligent' is derived from it for him, so it is determined to be an accident. Then either it is from the category of sciences or not, and the second is false, otherwise it would be described by that which does not know of inanimate objects and animals, so the first is determined, = 497. The Section of Sam'iyyat. Shaykh al-Islam said: It is an innate disposition by which one becomes prepared for perceiving theoretical sciences, and it is as if a light cast into the heart, end. And its locus is the heart, and its light is in the brain, as held by the two imams: Malik and... Then if it were theoretical, it would not be perceived except by the intellect, leading to infinite regress, so it is determined to be necessary. Then either all sciences are necessary, which is impossible, due to the refutation of some necessary things like the blind person, for the necessary things perceived by sight are absent from him while he is intelligent, so it is determined to be some necessary sciences. This is an explanation of what was supported by the words of Imam al-Haramayn and those with him, and it does not contradict the possibility that it is an accident concomitant to some sciences until its essence is established. And the truth is that it is the fruit of intellect, because it is the very knowledge, and it is only applied to sciences metaphorically because they are its fruit, just as a thing is known by its fruit. And some said: it is not from the category of necessary sciences, and defined it as an innate disposition – i.e., a nature and a faculty imprinted upon – by which knowledge of necessary things follows when the instruments are sound. And al-Shirazi defined it as: an attribute by which one distinguishes between good and evil, and this is the meaning of al-Shafi'i's saying: that it is the instrument of discrimination. As for those who said it is a substance – they are the philosophers – they defined it as: an essence conjoined with matter, abstract from it in its action, and it is the soul to which everyone points by saying 'I'. And in the words of Shaykh al-Islam, it is used homonymously for four meanings: for the innate disposition, for some necessary sciences, for sciences acquired through experience, and for the end of that power. And the truth is that it is spiritual, by which the soul perceives necessary and theoretical sciences. al-Shanawani. (1) His saying: (Shaykh al-Islam said) is not a branching from what came before, but an independent definition, so it would have been better to bring the waw, because his definition does not follow from the view that it is from the category of sciences. al-Shanawani. And Shaykh al-Islam is Zakariyya al-Ansari, whose biography has preceded. (2) His saying: (innate disposition) i.e., implanted, so it is from the category of faculties, and it is the sciences of al-Amir. According to al-Shanawani: i.e., implanted, i.e., an attribute and a power upon which the person is imprinted for perceiving theoretical sciences. (3) His saying: (And its locus is the heart and its light is in the brain) and this is the correct view according to al-Shafi'i and Malik. And as for its corruption by corruption of the brain... it does not indicate that it is its locus, as held by the philosophers and some jurists, because it is possible that soundness of the brain is a condition for the heart's connection to it by habit, even if its locus is the heart. al-Shanawani. According to al-Amir: if one is struck on the head and loses his intellect, then each has a separate blood-money, because the benefit is interwoven=
Source pages p. 498

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion on the reality and definition of intellect ('aql) within the framework of Sunni theology. It begins by addressing a line from the poem (Jawharat al-Tawhid) and then expands into a commentary. The commentator first notes that Ahl al-Sunna agree that intellect is an accident ('arad) and not a substance (jawhar), and most hold it to be from the category of sciences ('ulum). He then provides an extensive footnote explaining the disagreement with philosophers, Mu'tazila, and Khawarij who consider it a substance. The definition of al-Shafi'i is mentioned: the instrument of discrimination (ālat al-tamyīz). The commentator also cites al-Ghazali's view of a lordly subtlety (laṭīfa rabbāniyya) with three aspects: intellect, life, and soul. Then he enumerates five divisions of intellect: instinctive, acquired, bestowed, ascetic, and honorable (the Prophet's). Importantly, he asserts that knowledge ('ilm) is superior to intellect because it is an attribute of God, and that narrations praising intellect are fabricated. The discussion continues with a proof from Imām al-Ḥaramayn that intellect must be an accident belonging to some necessary sciences, avoiding infinite regress. After a section header 'Section of Sam'iyyat', Shaykh al-Islam (Zakariyya al-Ansari) defines intellect as an innate disposition (gharīza) for perceiving theoretical sciences, like a light cast into the heart. The commentator then explains the locus: the heart is the seat, but its light manifests in the brain. He refutes the philosophers' claim that the brain is the locus, arguing that brain health may be a condition for the heart's connection. The passage concludes with a note that 'aql is used homonymously for four meanings. Throughout, the commentator synthesizes earlier authorities and affirms the correct Sunni view that intellect is a spiritual accident by which the soul perceives necessary and theoretical knowledge.

Correction: Ahl al-Sunna hold it is an accident, as the text states clearly.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the position of Ahl al-Sunna regarding the nature of the intellect?
A) It is a substance (jawhar) like the philosophers say.
B) It is an accident ('arad) and from the category of sciences.
C) It is the soul itself, identical to the self.
D) It is a physical organ located in the brain.

Key Takeaway
Ahl al-Sunna unanimously affirm that intellect is an accident, not a substance, and it belongs to the category of sciences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your intellect is just an accident created by Allah and not your own substance, how will you change your daily habits of thinking and learning to reflect gratitude and humility?
?
Al-Ghazali says the true nature of your intellect is a 'lordly subtlety' known only to Allah. When you feel proud of your intelligence, what specific thought from this lesson will you recall to stay grounded?
?
The Sunni consensus says intellect is from the category of sciences—an accident that acquires. How will you ensure that what you acquire with your intellect is worthy of being called 'science' in Allah's sight? What one subject do you commit to study or teach with this intention starting today?

What Is Kalam?
beginner
4 min
The Five Types of Intellect and Superiority of Knowledge
Commentator enumerates five types of intellect and compares intellect with knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five categories of intellect and why knowledge is superior to intellect according to the commentator.

Core Idea
Intellect has five types, but knowledge is nobler because it is an attribute of Allah.

The commentator provides a detailed classification of intellect into five types: instinctive, acquired, bestowed, ascetic, and honorable. He then asserts that knowledge ('ilm) is better than intellect because it is one of Allah's attributes, and warns against fabricated hadith praising intellect.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the five types of intellect
Read the list carefully: غريزي (instinctive), كسبي (acquired), عطائي (bestowed), عقل الزهاد (ascetic), and شرفي (honorable – the Prophet's).
Note that the first two are common to believers and disbelievers.
2
Understand the superiority of knowledge
The commentator says 'العلم أفضل من العقل' because knowledge is an attribute of Allah, while intellect is created.
3
Recognize the fabrication
The text explicitly states that sayings praising intellect are fabricated, citing al-Suyuti.
This guards against overvaluing intellect over revelation.

The reality of the intellect. (Then look) in the books of the people (what they have explained) i.e., the interpretations and realities which they have clarified; because they are the subject matter for it, not in this poem, due to its small size. And the statements of Ahl al-Sunna are in agreement upon its being an accident, and most of them are that it is from the category of sciences. (1) His saying: (because they are the subject matter for it) is a cause for his saying: look in the books of the people. (2) In some copies: 'in this introduction'. (3) His saying: (upon its being an accident) i.e., that it is an accident, not a substance. Its opposite are the philosophers, who say it is a substance, and likewise the Mu'tazila and Khawarij. And some of them explained it as a substance by which the unseen things are perceived through intermediaries and the sensible things through observation, and some of them explained it otherwise. And al-Shafi'i explained it as the instrument of discrimination, and his explanation admits of both substantiality and accidentally. And in the words of al-Ghazali is what confirms that it is an abstract substance, and its summary is that there is a lordly subtlety known only to Allah, which in terms of its thinking is 'aql, in terms of the life of the body by it is life, and in terms of its desire and expression of it is soul, and the three are one in essence but different in consideration. And it is not said that every possessor of spirit necessarily has intellect, because the spirit is not intellect by itself, but by consideration of thinking. And know that the intellect is of five types: first: instinctive, which is in every human, believer and disbeliever; second: acquired, which is what a person acquires from associating with intelligent people, and it belongs to the believer and disbeliever as well; third: bestowed, which is the intellect of the believer by which he is guided to faith; fourth: the intellect of the ascetics, by which asceticism is attained; fifth: honorable, which is the intellect of our Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him), because it is the noblest of intellects. And knowledge is better than intellect because it is one of His attributes, and what is narrated about the virtue of intellect is fabricated with no basis, as stated by al-Jalal al-Suyuti. al-Shanawani. (4) In one copy: 'and the most important of them' which is wrong. So the meaning is that most of the sayings of Ahl al-Sunna are that intellect is from the category of sciences. · (5) His saying: (and most of them are that it is from the category of sciences) i.e., those who say it is an accident differed: some said it is from the category of sciences, and defined it as knowledge of some necessary sciences, which is the knowledge of the necessity of obligatories, the impossibility of impossibilities, and the possibility of possibilities, i.e., affirmation of them. Imam al-Haramayn and a group said: intellect is not a substance, because substances have attributes established for them and not established for others, and no name is derived from them for others. But intellect is an attribute established for the person, and the term 'intelligent' is derived from it for him, so it is determined to be an accident. Then either it is from the category of sciences or not, and the second is false, otherwise it would be described by that which does not know of inanimate objects and animals, so the first is determined, = 497. The Section of Sam'iyyat. Shaykh al-Islam said: It is an innate disposition by which one becomes prepared for perceiving theoretical sciences, and it is as if a light cast into the heart, end. And its locus is the heart, and its light is in the brain, as held by the two imams: Malik and... Then if it were theoretical, it would not be perceived except by the intellect, leading to infinite regress, so it is determined to be necessary. Then either all sciences are necessary, which is impossible, due to the refutation of some necessary things like the blind person, for the necessary things perceived by sight are absent from him while he is intelligent, so it is determined to be some necessary sciences. This is an explanation of what was supported by the words of Imam al-Haramayn and those with him, and it does not contradict the possibility that it is an accident concomitant to some sciences until its essence is established. And the truth is that it is the fruit of intellect, because it is the very knowledge, and it is only applied to sciences metaphorically because they are its fruit, just as a thing is known by its fruit. And some said: it is not from the category of necessary sciences, and defined it as an innate disposition – i.e., a nature and a faculty imprinted upon – by which knowledge of necessary things follows when the instruments are sound. And al-Shirazi defined it as: an attribute by which one distinguishes between good and evil, and this is the meaning of al-Shafi'i's saying: that it is the instrument of discrimination. As for those who said it is a substance – they are the philosophers – they defined it as: an essence conjoined with matter, abstract from it in its action, and it is the soul to which everyone points by saying 'I'. And in the words of Shaykh al-Islam, it is used homonymously for four meanings: for the innate disposition, for some necessary sciences, for sciences acquired through experience, and for the end of that power. And the truth is that it is spiritual, by which the soul perceives necessary and theoretical sciences. al-Shanawani. (1) His saying: (Shaykh al-Islam said) is not a branching from what came before, but an independent definition, so it would have been better to bring the waw, because his definition does not follow from the view that it is from the category of sciences. al-Shanawani. And Shaykh al-Islam is Zakariyya al-Ansari, whose biography has preceded. (2) His saying: (innate disposition) i.e., implanted, so it is from the category of faculties, and it is the sciences of al-Amir. According to al-Shanawani: i.e., implanted, i.e., an attribute and a power upon which the person is imprinted for perceiving theoretical sciences. (3) His saying: (And its locus is the heart and its light is in the brain) and this is the correct view according to al-Shafi'i and Malik. And as for its corruption by corruption of the brain... it does not indicate that it is its locus, as held by the philosophers and some jurists, because it is possible that soundness of the brain is a condition for the heart's connection to it by habit, even if its locus is the heart. al-Shanawani. According to al-Amir: if one is struck on the head and loses his intellect, then each has a separate blood-money, because the benefit is interwoven=

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion on the reality and definition of intellect ('aql) within the framework of Sunni theology. It begins by addressing a line from the poem (Jawharat al-Tawhid) and then expands into a commentary. The commentator first notes that Ahl al-Sunna agree that intellect is an accident ('arad) and not a substance (jawhar), and most hold it to be from the category of sciences ('ulum). He then provides an extensive footnote explaining the disagreement with philosophers, Mu'tazila, and Khawarij who consider it a substance. The definition of al-Shafi'i is mentioned: the instrument of discrimination (ālat al-tamyīz). The commentator also cites al-Ghazali's view of a lordly subtlety (laṭīfa rabbāniyya) with three aspects: intellect, life, and soul. Then he enumerates five divisions of intellect: instinctive, acquired, bestowed, ascetic, and honorable (the Prophet's). Importantly, he asserts that knowledge ('ilm) is superior to intellect because it is an attribute of God, and that narrations praising intellect are fabricated. The discussion continues with a proof from Imām al-Ḥaramayn that intellect must be an accident belonging to some necessary sciences, avoiding infinite regress. He refutes the philosophers' claim that the brain is the locus, arguing that brain health may be a condition for the heart's connection. What Is Kalam?
intermediate
6 min
Proof of Intellect as Accident and Definition as Innate Disposition
Commentator provides logical proof that intellect is an accident and defines it as innate disposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that intellect is an accident and the definition of intellect as an innate disposition for perceiving theoretical sciences.

Core Idea
Intellect must be an accident belonging to some necessary sciences, and it is an innate disposition (gharīza) for theoretical knowledge.

The commentator presents a logical argument from Imam al-Haramayn proving that intellect is an accident and specifically from the category of necessary sciences. Then Shaykh al-Islam defines intellect as an innate disposition, and the locus debate (heart vs brain) is discussed.

Guided Path
1
Follow the proof that intellect is an accident
Read the argument: intellect is an accident because it is a quality of a person, then it must be from sciences or not; if not, then inanimate objects would have it, so it must be from sciences.
This is a simple syllogism.
2
Understand the infinite regress argument
If intellect were theoretical, it would require another intellect ad infinitum, so it must be necessary. But not all sciences are necessary (e.g., blind person lacks visual necessary sciences), so it is some necessary sciences.
3
Examine Shaykh al-Islam's definition
Shaykh al-Islam (Zakariyya al-Ansari) defines intellect as an innate disposition (gharīza) for perceiving theoretical sciences, like a light in the heart.
4
Consider the locus of intellect
The commentator states the locus is the heart, while its light is in the brain. This opposes philosophers who say the brain is the locus.
The argument uses the example of brain injury not necessarily proving the brain is the locus.

فإما نظريا وهو لا يُدرك إلا بالعقل، فيلزم التسلسل، فتعين أنه ضروري، فإما جميع العلوم ضرورية وهو محال ، لنقض بعض الضروريات من نحو الأعمى، فإن الضروريات المدركة بالبصر منتفية عنه مع أنه عاقل ، فتعيَّن أنه بعض العلوم الضرورية ، هذا توضيح ما أيَّد به كلام إمام الحرمين ومن معه، وهو لا ينافي احتمال أنه عرض ملازم لبعض العلوم حتى يثبت عينها. والحق أنه ثمرة العقل، لأنه نفس العلم وإنما أطلق على العلوم مجازا من حيث إنها ثمرته ، كما يُعرف الشيء بثمرته . وبعضهم قال: إنه ليس من قبيل العلوم الضرورية، وعرَّفه بأنه غريزة - أي: طبيعة وملكة مطبوع عليها - يتبعها العلم بالضروريات عند سلامة الآلات وعرفه الشيرازي: بأنه صفة يميز بها بين الحسن والقبيح ، وهو معنى قول الشافعي: أنه آلة التمييز . وأمَّا مَن قال بجوهريته وهم الحكماء ، فعرفوه: بأنه ذات مقارن المادة في مجرد عن لها في فعله، وهو النفس التي يُشير إليها كل أحدٍ بقوله : أنا . وفي كلام شيخ الإسلام أنه يقال بالاشتراك لمعان أربعة للغريزة، ولبعض العلوم الضرورية، وللعلوم التي بالتجارب، ولانتهاء تلك القوة. والحق أنه روحاني، به تدرك النفس العلوم الضرورية

(۱) قوله: (قال شيخ الإسلام ليس مفرعا على ما قبله، بل تعريف مستقل ، فكان الأولى الإتيان بالواو ، لأن تعريفه لا يتأتى على القول بأنه من قبيل العلوم الشنواني. وشيخ الإسلام هو زكريا الأنصاري ، تقدمت ترجمته . (۲) قوله: (غريزة) أي مغروزةٌ ، فهو من قبيل الملكات، وهي علوم الأمير. وعند الشنواني: أي مغروزة ، أي : صفةٌ وقوة مطبوع عليها الشخص لدرك العلوم النظرية. The reality of the intellect. (Then look) in the books of the people (what they have explained) i.e., the interpretations and realities which they have clarified; because they are the subject matter for it, not in this poem, due to its small size. And the statements of Ahl al-Sunna are in agreement upon its being an accident, and most of them are that it is from the category of sciences. (1) His saying: (because they are the subject matter for it) is a cause for his saying: look in the books of the people. (2) In some copies: 'in this introduction'. (3) His saying: (upon its being an accident) i.e., that it is an accident, not a substance. Its opposite are the philosophers, who say it is a substance, and likewise the Mu'tazila and Khawarij. And some of them explained it as a substance by which the unseen things are perceived through intermediaries and the sensible things through observation, and some of them explained it otherwise. And al-Shafi'i explained it as the instrument of discrimination, and his explanation admits of both substantiality and accidentally. And in the words of al-Ghazali is what confirms that it is an abstract substance, and its summary is that there is a lordly subtlety known only to Allah, which in terms of its thinking is 'aql, in terms of the life of the body by it is life, and in terms of its desire and expression of it is soul, and the three are one in essence but different in consideration. And it is not said that every possessor of spirit necessarily has intellect, because the spirit is not intellect by itself, but by consideration of thinking. And know that the intellect is of five types: first: instinctive, which is in every human, believer and disbeliever; second: acquired, which is what a person acquires from associating with intelligent people, and it belongs to the believer and disbeliever as well; third: bestowed, which is the intellect of the believer by which he is guided to faith; fourth: the intellect of the ascetics, by which asceticism is attained; fifth: honorable, which is the intellect of our Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him), because it is the noblest of intellects. And knowledge is better than intellect because it is one of His attributes, and what is narrated about the virtue of intellect is fabricated with no basis, as stated by al-Jalal al-Suyuti. al-Shanawani. (4) In one copy: 'and the most important of them' which is wrong. So the meaning is that most of the sayings of Ahl al-Sunna are that intellect is from the category of sciences. · (5) His saying: (and most of them are that it is from the category of sciences) i.e., those who say it is an accident differed: some said it is from the category of sciences, and defined it as knowledge of some necessary sciences, which is the knowledge of the necessity of obligatories, the impossibility of impossibilities, and the possibility of possibilities, i.e., affirmation of them. Imam al-Haramayn and a group said: intellect is not a substance, because substances have attributes established for them and not established for others, and no name is derived from them for others. But intellect is an attribute established for the person, and the term 'intelligent' is derived from it for him, so it is determined to be an accident. Then either it is from the category of sciences or not, and the second is false, otherwise it would be described by that which does not know of inanimate objects and animals, so the first is determined, = 497. The Section of Sam'iyyat. Shaykh al-Islam said: It is an innate disposition by which one becomes prepared for perceiving theoretical sciences, and it is as if a light cast into the heart, end. And its locus is the heart, and its light is in the brain, as held by the two imams: Malik and... Then if it were theoretical, it would not be perceived except by the intellect, leading to infinite regress, so it is determined to be necessary. Then either all sciences are necessary, which is impossible, due to the refutation of some necessary things like the blind person, for the necessary things perceived by sight are absent from him while he is intelligent, so it is determined to be some necessary sciences. This is an explanation of what was supported by the words of Imam al-Haramayn and those with him, and it does not contradict the possibility that it is an accident concomitant to some sciences until its essence is established. And the truth is that it is the fruit of intellect, because it is the very knowledge, and it is only applied to sciences metaphorically because they are its fruit, just as a thing is known by its fruit. And some said: it is not from the category of necessary sciences, and defined it as an innate disposition – i.e., a nature and a faculty imprinted upon – by which knowledge of necessary things follows when the instruments are sound. And al-Shirazi defined it as: an attribute by which one distinguishes between good and evil, and this is the meaning of al-Shafi'i's saying: that it is the instrument of discrimination. As for those who said it is a substance – they are the philosophers – they defined it as: an essence conjoined with matter, abstract from it in its action, and it is the soul to which everyone points by saying 'I'. And in the words of Shaykh al-Islam, it is used homonymously for four meanings: for the innate disposition, for some necessary sciences, for sciences acquired through experience, and for the end of that power. And the truth is that it is spiritual, by which the soul perceives necessary and theoretical sciences. al-Shanawani. (1) His saying: (Shaykh al-Islam said) is not a branching from what came before, but an independent definition, so it would have been better to bring the waw, because his definition does not follow from the view that it is from the category of sciences. al-Shanawani. And Shaykh al-Islam is Zakariyya al-Ansari, whose biography has preceded. (2) His saying: (innate disposition) i.e., implanted, so it is from the category of faculties, and it is the sciences of al-Amir. According to al-Shanawani: i.e., implanted, i.e., an attribute and a power upon which the person is imprinted for perceiving theoretical sciences. (3) His saying: (And its locus is the heart and its light is in the brain) and this is the correct view according to al-Shafi'i and Malik. And as for its corruption by corruption of the brain... it does not indicate that it is its locus, as held by the philosophers and some jurists, because it is possible that soundness of the brain is a condition for the heart's connection to it by habit, even if its locus is the heart. al-Shanawani. According to al-Amir: if one is struck on the head and loses his intellect, then each has a separate blood-money, because the benefit is interwoven=
Source pages p. 498

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion on the reality and definition of intellect ('aql) within the framework of Sunni theology. It begins by addressing a line from the poem (Jawharat al-Tawhid) and then expands into a commentary. The commentator first notes that Ahl al-Sunna agree that intellect is an accident ('arad) and not a substance (jawhar), and most hold it to be from the category of sciences ('ulum). He then provides an extensive footnote explaining the disagreement with philosophers, Mu'tazila, and Khawarij who consider it a substance. The definition of al-Shafi'i is mentioned: the instrument of discrimination (ālat al-tamyīz). The commentator also cites al-Ghazali's view of a lordly subtlety (laṭīfa rabbāniyya) with three aspects: intellect, life, and soul. Then he enumerates five divisions of intellect: instinctive, acquired, bestowed, ascetic, and honorable (the Prophet's). Importantly, he asserts that knowledge ('ilm) is superior to intellect because it is an attribute of God, and that narrations praising intellect are fabricated. The discussion continues with a proof from Imām al-Ḥaramayn that intellect must be an accident belonging to some necessary sciences, avoiding infinite regress. After a section header 'Section of Sam'iyyat', Shaykh al-Islam (Zakariyya al-Ansari) defines intellect as an innate disposition (gharīza) for perceiving theoretical sciences, like a light cast into the heart. The commentator then explains the locus: the heart is the seat, but its light manifests in the brain. He refutes the philosophers' claim that the brain is the locus, arguing that brain health may be a condition for the heart's connection. The passage concludes with a note that 'aql is used homonymously for four meanings. Throughout, the commentator synthesizes earlier authorities and affirms the correct Sunni view that intellect is a spiritual accident by which the soul perceives necessary and theoretical knowledge.

Correction: Proof shows it is an accident, and its locus is the heart, with light in the brain.

Key Takeaway
Logical proof shows intellect is an accident from some necessary sciences, and its true definition is an innate disposition for theoretical knowledge with the heart as its locus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?
